Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures
close to terrain
A theoretical and practical analysis
Master Thesis
DTU Construct
Department of Civil and Mechanical Engineering

==PAGE:3==
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
Master Thesis
February 2025
By
Viktoria Visborg Thune
Cover photo:
Joanna Schalnat and Viktoria V. Thune, 2024
Published by:

==PAGE:4==
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
To reduce CO2-eq emissions from the construction sector, it is advisable to use more bio-based
materials in constructions. In this thesis, constructions with bio-based materials towards terrain
have been investigated, with a focus on highly ventilated crawl spaces and organic ground slabs
used in modular buildings, specifically with an intent to assess the risk of mould growth.
The work with the thesis has included an analysis of existing constructions, to examine cur-
rent practices, as well as interviews with actors in the construction industry, and hygrothermal
simulations of the two studied constructions. A large focus in the project has been to collect un-
published moisture measurements performed in existing constructions. Additionally, a life cycle
assessment of CO2-eq emissions associated with the constructions has been conducted, where it
was found that the use of an organic crawl space with screw foundations is the least impactful
solution.
One-dimensional hygrothermal simulations indicate that both studied constructions are moisture-
safe. However, the challenge is more complex, and the simulation model is very simplified. The
well-function of the construction should be validated by measurements. It proved to be more
difficult than expected to gather measurements from existing buildings.
The design, execution, as well as maintenance and operation of the constructions, are all crucial
aspects for the moisture-safety of the building. Lack of maintenance can lead to serious damages.
The constructions must be accessible for inspections, which can be done by installing moisture
sensors, but was found not to be complied with in most cases studied. The responsibility for per-
forming the measurements should be clearly communicated to the administrator of the building.
Additionally, a central system where continuous measurements are collected is recommended,
or alternatively, the expectations for administrators should be raised so that moisture measure-
ments are reported annually.
To build moisture-safe bio-based constructions towards terrain, several important factors must be
considered. This includes, among other things, choosing an appropriate location for the building,
implementing sufficient drainage based on gravitational principles, ensuring that the organic
materials in the construction are not in direct contact with soil, and maintaining the construction
according to the operations and maintenance plan. Furthermore, monitoring the construction
with moisture measurements should be carried out more consistently and effectively than at
present.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
iii
==PAGE:6==
Resume
For at reducere CO2 udledningen fra bygningssektoren er der et ønske om at anvende flere
biobaserede materialer i konstruktioner. I dette speciale er konstruktioner med biobasede mate-
rialer mod terræn blevet undersøgt, med fokus på kraftigt ventilerede krybekældre og organiske
terrændæk i modul byggeri, med særligt henblik på at vurdere risikoen for skimmelvækst.
Arbejdet med specialet har inkluderet en analyse af eksisterende konstruktioner for at undersøge
nuværende praksis, interviews med aktører i byggebranchen og hygrotermiske simuleringer af
de to undersøgte konstruktioner. Et stort fokus i projektet har været at indsamle upublicerede
fugtmålinger udført i eksisterende konstruktioner.
Desuden er der også foretaget en livscyclusvurdering af CO2e emissionerne forbundet med de
undersøgte konstruktioner, hvor det er fundet at anvendelsen af en organisk krybekælder med
skruefundamenter er den mindst belastende løsning.
En-dimensionelle hygrotermiske simuleringer indikerer, at begge konstruktioner er fugtsikre.
Udfordringen er imidlertid mere komplekst, og simuleringsmodellen er meget forenklet og bør
yderligere valideres ved målinger. Det har vist sig at være sværere end forventet at indsamle
målinger fra eksisterende byggerier.
Design og udførelsen af konstruktionerne samt drift og vedligehold har stor betydning for fugt-
sikkerheden af bygningen. Manglende vedligeholdelse kan føre til alvorlige skader. Konstruk-
tionerne skal være tilgængelige for undersøgelser, hvilket kan gøres ved hjælp af fugtsensorer,
men kravet blev konstateret ikke at blive fulgt i mange tilfælde. Ansvaret for at udføre målingerne
bør klart kommunikeres til administratoren af bygningen. Derudover anbefales et centralt sys-
tem hvor kontinuerlige målinger samles, eller alternativt at forventningerne til administratorerne
hæves, således at fugtmålinger skal rapporteres årligt.
For at bygge fugtsikre biobaserede konstruktioner mod terræn, skal flere vigtige faktorer overve-
jes. Dette inkluderer bl.a. valg af en passende placering for bygningen, implementering af dræn-
ing baseret på gravitation, sikring af at de organiske materialer i konstruktionen ikke er i direkte
kontakt med jord, og vedligeholdelse af konstruktionen i henhold til drifts- og vedligeholdelses-
planen. Derudover bør overvågning af konstruktionen med fugtmålinger udføres mere konsis-
tent og effektivt.

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:13==
1
Introduction
Traditionally, the crawl space construction has been widely used in Scandinavia, and was ini-
tially a moisture-safe solution for constructions towards terrain. However, in the 1970’s an in-
creased demand for energy-efficient buildings led to higher insulation requirements for building
envelopes, including the suspended floor above crawl spaces. Many original crawl spaces were
retrofitted with insulation, to meet demands for reduced heat loss. Unfortunately, more insulated
and airtight suspended floors resulted in moisture issues within the crawl spaces, leading to a
shift towards constructing concrete ground slabs instead. These issues will further be elaborated
in the literature review.
Moisture is considered the primary cause of building damages. In large quantities and over ex-
tended periods, high levels of moisture in constructions can lead to deformation of components,
decay of organic materials due to rot, and mould growth on material surfaces. Mould growth
has a significant negative impact on people’s health, and damages of mould growth must be
effectively remedied. Therefore, it is critical to build moisture-safe constructions.
In 2007, KAB, a housing administrator in Copenhagen, developed the AlmenBolig+ (SocialHous-
ing+) framework to achieve cheaper rent for social housing. To keep costs low, residents are
responsible for maintaining common areas and the exterior of the buildings [1]. Part of this con-
cept involves prefabricating buildings as modules, which are delivered and assembled on-site.
This approach shortens the building process and reduces costs further. Most prefabricated mod-
ules are produced by Scandi Byg and BM Byggeindustri, with the first project completed in 2011
[1, 2, 3]. The modules are based on wooden elements and include a solution towards terrain
consisting of wooden cassettes. For quick assembly, buildings are often constructed on a crawl
space foundation or placed directly onto foundations and expanded polystyrene, referred to as
organic ground slab constructions in this thesis. These constructions are often below ground level
to ensure level access, which is required by Danish regulation [4]. The crawl spaces for modular
buildings are highly ventilated.
In Denmark, the building sector accounts for 40% of the resource consumption as well as 40%
of the energy use [5]. A significant amount of energy, resulting in high CO2 emissions, is used
in the production of building materials, especially the mineral based materials, such as concrete.
The production of cement alone contributes to 7% of global CO2 emissions [6]. Therefore, there
has in the latest years been a focus on reducing the emissions from the building sector, in order
for Denmark to comply with the Paris Agreement, which aims to limit global warming to below
1.5 degrees [7]. Since January 2023 there has been regulation on all new buildings above 1000 m2
to have emissions below 12 kg CO2/m2 per year in Denmark [8]. This figure will be decreased
to an average of 7.1 kg CO2/m2 per year for all new buildings from July of 2025 [9], and will be
further reduced towards 2029.
Bio-based materials, such as construction wood, have lower CO2 emissions than mineral based
products, especially in the production stage. This is due to photosynthesis, where the plants
use sunlight to convert water and CO2 into sugar, meaning that carbon is stored in the tissue.
Furthermore, the production of bio-based building materials require less energy than the mineral-
based materials used widely today. Given the current challenges with CO2 emissions in the
construction industry, there is a growing focus on using bio-based materials in construction as a
substitute for mineral materials. This gives a reason to explore moisture-safe ways to construct
crawl spaces using bio-based products, rather than concrete ground slabs. Ensuring the moisture-
safety of organic constructions built against terrain is crucial for reducing CO2 emissions, as
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
1
==PAGE:14==
renovating damaged constructions would lead to additional emissions.
This thesis focuses on modular buildings produced by BM Byggeindustri and Scandi Byg that are
administered by KAB. It aims to provide an overview of the current use of organic constructions
towards terrain, investigating if it is possible to build bio-based constructions against terrain
with low risk of mould growth, when requirements for ventilation, insulation, maintenance,
and level access among other aspects are met. This is investigated through a review of exist-
ing constructions, interviews with experts in the building sector, and hygrothermal simulations.
Hygrothermal simulations can indicate the moisture safety of constructions, but they must be
supplemented with moisture measurements from existing buildings to assess their practical per-
formance. Additionally, the environmental impacts of the organic constructions are estimated
and compared to the alternative of constructing concrete ground slabs. This thesis focuses on
social housing and modular constructions, but the findings can be extended to other areas where
crawl spaces are used.
2
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:15==
2
Literature review
The literature review includes both a historical overview and an summarization of existing liter-
ature. The purpose of the historical overview is to gain insight into the regulations concerning
crawl spaces and explain why crawl spaces are no longer a recommended solution. The historical
overview primarily focuses on new constructions, despite a significant portion of the literature
focusing on retrofitted crawl spaces. Recommendations related to retrofitting are mostly ex-
cluded due to the scope of the thesis. The purpose of the literature review is to investigate recent
research on crawl spaces and summarize the findings. This serves as a guideline for further
exploration.
2.1
Historical overview
The historical overview is conducted by examining all the historical SBi guidelines, leaflets, spe-
cial prints and other documents addressing moisture in buildings, and compiling information
regarding construction of crawl spaces. The primary database used for accessing historical lit-
erature is anvisninger.dk [10]. Furthermore, a list of SBi publications from 1947 to 1997 has
been used to gain an overview [11], and historical papers not available on anvisninger.dk has
been sourced elsewhere. Erfaringsblade (experience leaflets) from Fonden BYG-ERFA are also
included [12].
Both SBi guidelines and experience leaflets from BYG-ERFA are a part of the required common
technical knowledge, and are guidelines for best practice in the building industry in Denmark.
The parties of the construction industry are obliged to be familiar with the required common
technical knowledge, and it is also a part of the basis for resolution of disputes [13]. The SBi
guidelines are based on knowledge from research and technical insight and experiences, and are
written by the Department of the Built Environment at University of Aalborg (BUILD) [13]. SBi
guidelines and BYG-ERFA experience leaflets are not legislation and are therefore voluntary to
follow. It is permitted to choose other solutions than the ones described in the required common
technical knowledge, however it should be documented that these decisions are well-founded,
i.e., at least as good as what is described in the required common technical knowledge [12].
The historical overview shows, that knowledge about the consequences of prior recommenda-
tions has been gathered over time. Some recommendations have been withdrawn due to unfore-
seen outcomes which led to moisture damages.
2.1.1
The traditional crawl space
In Denmark, there has been a long tradition of building ventilated crawl spaces. Generally, the
traditional crawl space is constructed to create distance between the terrain and the floor, to
prevent direct contact between the wooden beams in the floor and the ground moisture. This
reduces the risk of rot and mould attacks.
The traditional cold crawl spaces are ventilated by outside air, for instance by ventilation open-
ings in the facade, and the insulation is placed in the suspended floor above the crawl space, as
shown in Figure 2.1 from SBi Guideline 7 ”Moisture and Insulation” from 1957. At the bottom of
the crawl space, a capillary barrier is made, and in some cases, a concrete layer is placed on top
to prevent the moisture in the ground from rising into the crawl space. Due to the ventilation,
the radon rising from the ground into the crawl space is diluted [14].
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
3
==PAGE:16==
Figure 2.1: Illustration of traditional crawl space, from SBi Guideline 7 [15]. The figure is translated from
Danish. The crawl space is ventilated by outside air through grilles in the facades. The minimum height
of the crawl space cavity is 40 cm, and at the bottom of the cavity there is an asphalt mat for moisture
protection.
The possible moisture sources for crawl spaces are listed below, from SBi Guideline 279 [14].
• Moisture from other building materials during construction
• Surface water penetrating trough leaks in the foundation or low grates
• Moisture from the outside air ventilated into the crawl space
• Ground moisture, for instance rising damp
• Moisture from above rooms
• Leaks in the installations in the crawl space
The hygrothermal conditions in a crawl space are different between winter and summer. In
winter the temperature inside the traditional crawl space is a little warmer than outside. When
outside air is ventilated into a crawl space the air is heated, which leads to a decrease in relative
humidity. See more about this in section 3.1.1. On the other hand, in the summer, the surfaces
in the crawl space are colder than the outside air. This leads to the air being cooled down when
ventilated into the crawl space, which causes the relative humidity to increase. It can be a concern
that the relative humidity will rise above the limit for mould growth, which is normally set to
75% relative humidity [16], see more in section 3.2.
The first guideline on the subject is the SBi Guideline 7 ”Moisture and Insulation” from 1957
[15]. The guideline advises among other things, to ensure sufficient ventilation by placing the
crawl space vents no more than 7 meters apart in the same wall. A minimum of one vent with
a minimum area of 10 x 15 cm has to be present in every wall. Furthermore, the suspended
floors should be well insulated to ensure the occupants’ comfort, and the ground should have a
vapour barrier, which could be either a layer of concrete, an asphalt mat, or polyethylene film.
Moreover, the height of the crawl space should be a minimum of 40 cm. Additionally, the terrain
surrounding the building should have a sufficient slope away from the building.
Later publications align with this, e.g. the SBi moisture brochure ”Moisture and Crawl Spaces”
from 1973 [17], which refers to the Building Regulations 1972 [18]. In this guideline the minimum
height is increased, preferred to be 60-80 cm, but minimum 50 cm, to ensure access to the crawl
space is more convenient. The slope away from the building is now advised to be minimum 1:50.
4
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:17==
Also the SBi Guideline 139 ”Moisture insulation of buildings” from 1984 aligns with this [19]. A
difference is that the distance between the vents is reduced to 6 meters, leading to increased ven-
tilation. Furthermore, the suspended floor above the crawl space should have a vapour barrier. A
vapour barrier limits the amount of moisture from the inside air, that is transported by diffusion
through the construction, see more in section 3.1.4.
In the SBi special print 170 “A New Crawl Space Construction” from 1967, a new method for
constructing crawl spaces is proposed and tested [20]. The crawl space is weakly ventilated and
heated by uninsulated pipes, and there is no insulation between the crawl space and the occupied
rooms above. The moisture control was found to be adequate, but the research revealed that the
ventilation of the crawl space was higher than expected, which was considered the reason for
the construction’s good performance. Due to the lack of insulation there was a significant heat
loss, double the amount of the normal construction.
In general for traditional crawl spaces, the heat loss from the rooms above the crawl space and
well-insulated pipes inside the crawl space, is significant. In the SBi Guideline 111 ”Thermal
insulation of buildings” from 1986 it is stated that even for insulated heat pipes underneath the
insulation in a normally ventilated crawl space, the heat loss can be almost half of the building’s
total heat requirement [21].
It says in the SBi moisture leaflets from 1973 in section 5 ”Moisture and crawl spaces” that ”a well-
executed crawl space is a secure construction, in terms of moisture control, and it should be used
instead of a ground slab in all cases where there is suspicion that the soil is particularly damp or
that incoming surface water could cause moisture problems” [17]. Another reason to prefer crawl
spaces over ground slabs at this point in time, is that it is easier to repair the construction or
installations in the crawl space.
2.1.2
The ’70s oil crisis led to recommendations for additional insulation
In 1973, the international oil crisis had a major influence on Denmark. The oil prices sky-
rocketed, and since the Danish energy consumption was 90% dependent on oil [22], it led to
an urgent need to save energy and consequently, a demand for better insulation in both new
and existing buildings [23]. In February of 1974, the magazine Samvirke recommended insulat-
ing cavities in homes, such as crawl spaces [24]. The crisis extended into the 80s and led to a
number of guidelines concerning insulation.
The SBi Guideline 100 ”Insulate now” from 1979 states that by increasing the insulation towards
the ground (for instance, below the suspended floor in crawl spaces), the heat loss would be
reduced, leading to energy savings [25]. It is mentioned, that the crawl space should be well
ventilated to avoid moisture damages.
SBi report 162 ”Building renovation combined with retrofitting insulation” from 1985 states that
additional insulation should always be installed when renovating roofs and crawl spaces [26].
The reason was to save energy and reduce or eliminate the discomfort due to draft. It is stated
that moisture issues can be resolved by installing perimeter drainage. In general, retrofitting of
crawl spaces were very highly encouraged.
However, in both SBi Guideline 139 from 1984 and SBi Guideline 178 from 1993 with the same
title “Moisture insulation of buildings”, it is mentioned that increased insulation thicknesses leads
to higher relative humidity than before, and therefore there is a risk of mould growth and rot in
the wooden beams [19, 27]. It is stated that it is necessary to take measures against evaporation of
ground moisture, especially if the floor is constructed as wooden beams. However, the guidelines
both state, that retrofitting by placing a layer of insulation on the bottom side of the suspended
floor rarely causes moisture problems, if the insulation material is vapour-permeable.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
5
==PAGE:18==
2.1.3
Consequences of higher insulation thicknesses in crawl spaces
Through the years it has become evident that moisture related issues have occurred due to the
retrofitting of crawl spaces and construction of new crawl spaces with higher insulation thick-
nesses. In the BYG-ERFA experience leaflet ”Moisture investigations of buildings” from 2023, it
is written that ”moisture can lead to serious damages in both ground slabs and crawl spaces. In
retrofitted floors above crawl spaces there is often mould growth on the wood” [16].
In the experience leaflet ”Moisture problems and odor issues in externally ventilated crawl spaces”
from 2002 [28], it is stated that for buildings with mould problems in the crawl space, often all
the recommendations for ventilation have been followed, and the only issue has been that the
construction is highly insulated. This applies to both original and retrofitted constructions. This
is because the moisture safety of crawl spaces rely on the significant heat loss from the above
rooms, to raise the temperature in the crawl space and thereby lower the relative humidity. The
heat loss has been reduced significantly due to the requirements for better insulation, reducing
the temperature in the crawl space. This can lead to a higher relative humidity, which may
provide favourable conditions for mould growth if organic materials are present.
In the experience leaflet from 2002, it is not recommended to use traditional cold crawl spaces in
new constructions. It is instead recommended to build either a concrete ground slab or heated
crawl space with minimal ventilation, see section 2.2.2. It is not recommended to change a con-
struction that is functioning, meaning without moisture issues. It is not recommended to use
organic materials in the construction towards the crawl space.
The experience leaflet ”Risks of externally ventilated crawl spaces” from 2009 replaces the previ-
ous experience leaflet from 2002 [29]. It is still not recommended to construct traditional cold
crawl spaces, instead ground slabs or warm crawl spaces are recommended. The tone is more
pronounced in this experience leaflet. In the first leaflet from 2002, it is ”not recommended” to
construct outdoor ventilated crawl spaces, whereas in the 2009 leaflet, the use of outdoor air
ventilated crawl spaces is ”advised against”. It is recommended to insulate the foundations to
enhance the heat and moisture conditions. Wood or wood-based construction materials should
be avoided.
In SBi Guideline 239 “Retrofitting insulation of small houses - energy savings and planning” from
2012 it is also concluded that the traditional cold crawl space is problematic [30]. The crawl
space is therefore no longer a good and moisture-safe solution. It is written in the guideline
that the most energy-efficient and moisture-safe, however also the most extensive solution, is to
rebuild the crawl space into a ground floor slab. However, if retrofitting is desired, the guideline
states that much stronger ventilation must be established. The ventilation openings should be
increased to be 1/50 of the ground floor area.
2.2
Current recommendations concerning crawl spaces
The most recent guideline concerning moisture in crawl spaces is SBi Guideline 279 ”Moisture in
buildings - moisture in construction components” from 2023 [14]. Three possible constructions for
crawl spaces are described in the guideline; traditional cold crawl space, warm crawl space, and
highly ventilated crawl space. It is stated that the traditional crawl space should only be used
if there are no other possibilities. The guideline states that it is safer to construct warm crawl
spaces, see section 2.2.2, where the crawl space is ventilated with air from the above rooms. An-
other alternative is a highly ventilatied crawl space, see section 2.2.3. For the highly ventilated
construction there is no accumulation of moisture, since it follows the outside conditions. Other
forms of the cold crawl space may only be used, when hygrothermal calculations or measure-
ments have proven that the moisture and temperature conditions are safe [14]. The guideline
states that crawl spaces are not preferable to ground slabs when considering moisture safety.
6
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:19==
2.2.1
Traditional cold crawl spaces
Traditional cold crawl spaces should be avoided in new constructions, due to, as mentioned
before, the present requirements for insulation can lead to moisture damages [14]. If however, a
traditional crawl space is chosen as the solution, the layers towards the crawl space should consist
of only inorganic materials. It is advised against using organic materials in the construction at
all. If organic materials are used in a cold crawl space, they should be protected from moisture by
placing at least 100 mm of non-moisture absorbing insulation below the organic material. This
should prevent that the relative humidity increases too much near the organic material. It can
be an advantage to insulate the bottom and sides of the crawl space lightly.
The recommendation for the ventilation is that the ventilation area should be at least 1/500 of the
ground floor area. The ventilation openings should be placed with maximum 6 meters between,
and the ventilation area should be minimum of 150 cm2, where the canals are assumed to be
straight. The ventilation should be increased, if the canals are bent. To ensure radon protection,
the suspended floor construction should be made as air-tight as possible. At the bottom of the
crawl space cavity there should be a layer of concrete on top of a capillary barrier. This is to
prevent the rise of ground moisture, which reduces the relative humidity.
An inorganic board should be placed below the beam/insulation layer (called beam layer going
forward), which can act as a wind barrier. A wind barrier is used to avoid air flow in the insu-
lation material, which would reduce the insulation efficiency [31]. It is important that the wind
barrier has a small diffusion resistance factor compared to that of the vapour barrier, generally
the diffusion resistance should be 10 times smaller than that of the vapour barrier. An illustration
of the traditional crawl space can be seen in Figure 2.2. The illustration is from SBi Guideline
279 [14].
Figure 2.2: Illustration of current recommendations for a traditional crawl space, from SBi Guideline 279
[14]. The figure is translated from Danish. A vapour barrier should be placed on the warm side of the
insulation layer. An inorganic board should be placed below the beam layer, and beneath this a minimum
of 100 mm layer of inorganic insulation, if organic materials are used in the construction. On the bottom
of the crawl space cavity a concrete slab should be placed on top of capillary barrier. The total ventilation
area should be at least 1/500 of the ground floor area, and ventilation openings should be minimum 150
cm2 per 6 meters.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
7
==PAGE:20==
2.2.2
Warm crawl spaces
To limit the problems with high relative humidity in traditional cold crawl spaces, it is recom-
mended to construct a warm crawl space. A warm crawl space has no insulation towards the
heated rooms above the crawl space and therefore have almost the same temperature as these
rooms. In a warm crawl space, the walls and bottom floor are different from a traditional crawl
space, since they should be more insulated and air-tight. The foundations should be insulated
as basement walls, and the bottom slab should be insulated as a ground slab. The bottom floor
should be consisting of a capillary barrier at the bottom, an insulation layer and a concrete slab
above, as shown in Figure 2.3.
In a warm crawl space, the cavity is ventilated with air from the above rooms, and the ventilation
should be mechanical, and connected to the ventilation of the rest of the building. The air change
rate should be the same as the rest of the house, around 0.5 ach (air changes per hour). It should
be ensured that there is a slight under-pressure in the crawl space, causing air to leak from the
above rooms through natural leakages or grates in the floor.
An illustration of the warm crawl space can be seen in Figure 2.3. The illustration is from SBi
Guideline 279 [14].
Figure 2.3: Illustration of recommendations for a warm crawl space, from SBi Guideline 279 [14]. The
figure is translated from Danish. There is no insulation between the heated rooms and the crawl space.
The foundations are insulated as basement walls, and the bottom slab should be insulated as a ground
slab, both constructions should be air-tight. The ventilation should be mechanical.
2.2.3
Highly ventilated cold crawl spaces
The third possible solution for a crawl space construction is a highly ventilated cold crawl space.
In a highly ventilated crawl space the ventilation area should be 1/50 of the ground floor area,
which is 10 times more than the traditional recommendations. Moisture is not accumulating in
the crawl space because the conditions inside are almost the same as outside due to the high
ventilation. This solution leads to an increased heat loss, since the thermal capacity of the soil is
not utilized, and therefore the solution is less favourable from an energy-efficiency perspective.
If wooden beams are used, they should be protected from moisture by placing at least 100 mm
8
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:21==
insulation below. Organic material can be used in the construction, however the organic material
should not be exposed to the crawl space. If however, wood is exposed to the bottom side, it
should be pressure-treated.
The bottom of the crawl space can be constructed with a capillary barrier of concrete, or by
leveled soil with a robust membrane to act as a moisture barrier, which can be kept in place by a
layer of 50 mm gravel.
An illustration of the highly ventilated crawl space can be seen in Figure 2.4. The illustration is
from SBi Guideline 279 [14]. Some of the aspects of the highly ventilated crawl space are also
covered in the experience leaflet ”Slab above ground in pavilion construction” [32].
Figure 2.4: Illustration of recommendations for a highly ventilated crawl space, from SBi Guideline 279
[14]. The figure is translated from Danish. The ventilation area should be at least 1/50 of the ground floor
area. If the load-carrying structure are wooden beams, a layer of 100 mm inorganic insulation should be
placed underneath. At the bottom a moisture barrier can be placed on leveled soil, kept in place by 50 mm
gravel.
2.2.4
Light ground slabs
Another possibility for a construction towards terrain is the ground slab. The ground slab is a
compact construction without ventilation by outdoor air. A traditional ground slab consists of
concrete, however light ground slabs can be made as well, where the load-distributing plate can
be a chipboard for floors, see Figure 2.5. The construction of a light ground slab can be a layer
of gravel, a capillary barrier and thermal insulating layer of cellular plastic, such as expanded
polystyrene (EPS).
There should be taken measures to ensure against radon infiltration. Furthermore, it is recom-
mended to install perimeter drainage, especially when there is significant water impact on the
exterior of the ground slab.
An illustration of a light ground slab can be seen in Figure 2.5. The illustration is from SBi
Guideline 279 [14].
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
9
==PAGE:22==
Figure 2.5: Illustration of recommendations for a light ground slab, from SBi Guideline 279 [14]. The figure
is translated from Danish. The load-distributing plate can be chipboard.
2.2.5
Other recommendations regarding crawl spaces
2.2.5.1
Wet rooms
The SBi Guideline 279 recommends that below wet rooms, e.g. bathrooms, the suspended floor
should be constructed by concrete or aerated concrete. When the crawl space is inaccessible, due
to a low height of the cavity, it is not allowed to use organic materials in the construction below
the wet room.
2.2.5.2
Level access
The Danish Building Regulations §51 set a requirement for level access to all types of buildings
[8]. The requirements for level access is described in BYG-ERFA’s experience leaflet ”Level access
and ground slab” from 2022 [4]. Level access can be difficult to provide when using a crawl space
construction. It is possible to use ramps instead of having the slab or suspended floor in level
with the terrain, but this solution has high demands for the surrounding terrain. The slope of
the ramps may be maximum 1:20. In front of all doors a level area of at least 1.5 x 1.5 meters is
required.
Level access can lead to a risk of moisture related issues, if the surrounding terrain is sloping
towards the building, if the soil has become compacted during the construction or if the soil is
unsuitable for infiltration.
Often the terrain is in level with the indoor floor, so ramps are avoided. To avoid moisture
accumulation around the plinth and ground slab, a plinth trench (Danish: sokkelrende) should
be established [4]. The depth of the plinth trench should be at least 150 mm. Furthermore, to
prevent water ingress into the floor construction, it is recommended that the upper surface of
the concrete slab should be at least 50 mm above the surrounding terrain, i.e. the bottom of the
plinth trench, see Figure 2.6. The experience leaflet assumes that the ground slab is made of
concrete.
10
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:23==
Figure 2.6: Illustration of a plinth trench, from BYG-ERFA experience leaflet (19) 22 08 18 [4]. The depth
of the trench should be 150 mm and the upper side of the concrete slab should be at least 50 mm above
surrounding terrain. There are various other possible constructions in the leaflet.
2.2.5.3
Organic material below terrain
SBi Guideline 279 states that where there is a risk of flooding during heavy rain, the suspended
floor in a cold, outdoor-ventilated crawl space should be placed at least 150 mm above ground
level. In case the crawl space fills with water, the suspended floor can become damp and moisture-
damaged. It is stated that organic materials in particular should be placed above ground level.
2.2.5.4
Height of accessible crawl space
The term ”crawl space” is used for all constructions with air below the suspended floor. If the
crawl space should actually be accessible, for instance for maintenance of the construction or
pipes, the height is required to be at least 600 mm according to ”Industry guidelines for working
in crawl spaces” [33], however it is recommended to be 800 mm [14].
Installations placed in the crawl space must be protected against frost damage and should be
serviceable. Often, the distance between the bottom of the suspended floor and the gravel layer is
only about 200 mm, making the crawl space inaccessible [14]. The installations can be servicable
from above through hatches in the floor, in which case the requirement for free working height
is waived.
2.2.5.5
Water safety measures
Ground slabs and crawl spaces should only be used where the terrain is suitable, meaning there is
no risk of the ground water rising to a level that reaches into the capillary barrier. In the bottom
of the crawl space there should be a capillary barrier to prevent moisture being sucked up to the
bottom of the crawl space. There should always be made perimeter drainage, unless the soil is
well-drained, so that infiltrating surface water can be removed.
In case of climatic events like cloud bursts, it should be made sure that water that gets into the
crawl space can be removed, for instance pumped out. Furthermore, the terrain surrounding the
building should be constructed with a slope away from the building of minimum 1:40 for around
3 meters, so that water is led away from the building.
For buildings placed in low-level areas, and areas with risk of floods, it is recommended to raise
the ground slab and only use inorganic materials in the floor construction. If the surrounding
area is sloping towards the building, a drain or a plinth trench should be made to ensure that
water will not reach the foundation. A ramp is seen as a more safe solution compared to the
plinth trench. The plinth trench should be cleaned at least once a year to ensure functionality.
Mould growth in new constructions can be caused by the materials being too wet for too long,
which can be mitigated with a moisture strategy plan, which is described in the experience leaflet
”Moisture strategy in construction projects for the prevention of mould growth” [34]. A moisture
strategy plan should contain information about how to deal with moisture during the construc-
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
11
==PAGE:24==
tion, for instance protection against moisture from the weather, and the drying process of mate-
rials. This is not a part of the scope of this project, and will not be discussed further.
2.3
State-of-the-art
After the turn of the millennium, several studies have examined crawl spaces. In the literature
various types of crawl spaces have been investigated, and they will be outlined in this section.
In this section there will be a focus on indoor and outdoor air ventilated crawl spaces, ground
moisture evaporation and air change rate.
The paper ”Crawl spaces in wood framed single family dwellings in Sweden: unwanted yet popular”
from 2007 [35], is used to outline different possible constructions. The paper is a literature review
of the types of crawl spaces used in Sweden and the respective advantages and disadvantages.
The paper is also based on interviews with engineers. Five types of crawl spaces are used in
Sweden;
• Plinth foundation
• Outdoor air ventilated crawl space (either naturally or mechanically ventilated)
• Indoor air ventilated crawl space (mechanically ventilated)
• Unventilated crawl space
• Suspended foundation
For the suspended foundation the building sits on top of large stones, but this is no longer an
option for new buildings.
The plinth foundation is a building resting on pillars with an open space below the building,
which means that the environment under the building is like that of the outdoor environment.One
of the advantages of this type is that it is possible to place over any type of terrain. If the building
is left open and conditions are like the outdoor environment, there is very little risk of mould
or moisture damage. The disadvantage of this type of construction is that it is harder to comply
with accessibility demands, since the floor is high above ground.
For outdoor air ventilated crawl spaces, as Figure 2.2, the paper says that the advantages are
accessibility for maintenance, repairs, and inspection. The disadvantage is that the construction
performs badly during the warmer months of the year. The paper’s authors are ”amazed” that
companies in Sweden are still using this solution, and the paper states that in some cases, these
new buildings have mould growth within five years. The paper states that in an interview with a
company, the company answered that they use the outdoor air ventilated construction, because
it is the cheapest way for them to mount a prefabricated single-family home.
The paper defines indoor air ventilated crawl spaces as described for the warm crawl space in
section 2.2.2, see Figure 2.3. An advantage of indoor air ventilated crawl spaces is that there is
very little chance for moisture problems occurring if it is ensured that ground water will not be
a problem. Another advantage is that the design can ensure a higher floor temperature, thereby
enhancing the thermal comfort of occupants. A disadvantage of the construction is that it must
be ensured that the ventilation is working at all times; otherwise, the system will fail since it relies
on mechanical exhaust and/or air supply [35]. This puts a high demand on the homeowner. The
paper concludes that if designed and constructed correctly, the indoor air ventilated crawl space
can be a cheaper alternative with many advantages over an outdoor air ventilated crawl space
[35].
If an unventilated crawl space is used, it is found that it must be without organic material in the
construction since using organic materials will lead to moisture problems such as mould.
12
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:25==
2.3.1
Ventilation by indoor air
The paper ”Highly insulated crawl spaces with controlled minimal ventilation - proof of concept by
field measurements” from 2020 [36], presents measurements made on four existing constructions
with non-accessible crawl spaces. The buildings are placed in Southern Sweden, and the mea-
surements were made over one year. The crawl spaces are highly insulated, and in the described
concept there must be more insulation against the soil than under the suspended floor. An ex-
haust fan ventilated the crawl space mechanically, creating a pressure difference between the
crawl space and the above rooms. An illustration from the paper can be seen in Figure 2.7.
Figure 2.7: Rendering from ”Highly insulated crawl spaces with controlled minimal ventilation - proof of
concept by field measurements” [36]. The crawl space is ventilated minimally by mechanical exhaust ven-
tilation, creating a lower pressure in the crawl space compared to the above rooms.
The results showed low relative humidities, mostly below 75% with a few exceptions. The paper
recommends this practice for all buildings with wooden floors. The buildings had very good air
tightness of the base floor and crawl space. This is important, since this along with mechan-
ical ventilation enables a slightly lower air-pressure of the crawl space compared to indoors.
The paper states that a small under-pressure prevents flow of radon and fungal spores to the
above rooms, citing the paper ”Radon, fungal spores and MVOCs reduction in crawl space house: A
case study and crawl space development by hygrothermal modelling” [37] (MVOCs are Microbial
Volatile Organic Compounds).
Due to high heat capacity of the ground and foundations for this construction, there is a time
lag between the outdoor temperatures and the temperature inside the crawl space. Therefore, it
takes time for the temperature in the crawl space to become equal with the outside air. The time
lag can cause significant increases in the relative humidity [36].
In the paper ”Radon, fungal spores and MVOCs reduction in crawl space house: A case study and
crawl space development by hygrothermal modelling” from 2018 [37], investigations were con-
ducted on a single-family house in Finland to examine how ventilation of the crawl space af-
fected the concentrations of radon, fungal spores, and MVOCs. The crawl space is illustrated in
Figure 2.8.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
13
==PAGE:26==
Figure 2.8: Illustration of the investigated crawl space and foundation in ”Radon, fungal spores and MVOCs
reduction in crawl space house: A case study and crawl space development by hygrothermal modelling” [37].
The house originally had a ventilation system with an exhaust fan from the indoors to the crawl
space, where indoor air was blown into the crawl space and let outdoors through a duct in the
roof. This system was remodelled to be two separate systems so that the crawl space had a sepa-
rate two-way mechanical ventilation system (exhaust and supply fans). It is written in the paper,
that the exhaust system from the indoors to the crawl space was efficient to prevent radon flow,
however high biological concentrations were detected in the crawl space before the redesign,
since moist and warm air was blown into the space. The separate system created a decrease in
the concentration of fungal spores.
In the paper ”Experimental investigation of a crawl space located in a sub-arctic climate” from 2020
[38] measurements were conducted in a crawl space on Sweden with a dehumidifier installed.
The crawl space is ventilated by supply air with previously heated air from a garage and the
crawl space has no additional openings to the outside, see illustration of the plan of the crawl
space with location of dehumidifier and measurements points in Figure 2.9.
Figure 2.9: Illustration of the crawl space investigated in ”Experimental investigation of a crawl space located
in a sub-arctic climate” [38]. The illustration shows the location of the dehumidifier and measurements
points.
14
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:27==
It was found that, when using a dehumidifier in the crawl space, the humidity was below the
critical point for the whole year. Simulations without a dehumidifier predict that mould growth
will happen under the supply air ventilation conditions (with a mould index of up to 6). For a
naturally ventilated crawl space (1.5 ach) without a dehumidifier, simulations showed that the
conditions are critical (with a mould index of up to 4), and it is predicted that mould will start to
grow. For a definition of mould index see section 3.3.1.2. Furthermore, the measurements in the
crawl space show, that it is unnecessary to measure at several points in the crawl space, since
the difference between the measurements is small.
2.3.2
Ventilation by outdoor air
The paper ”Moisture related problems in highly insulated constructions (literature studies, measure-
ments and calculations)” from 2017 [39], contains one year measurements of a crawl space located
in Denmark with two different designs; part of the crawl space was insulated with a 50 mm layer
of mineral wool beneath the suspended floor, and the other part was not insulated below, as seen
in Figure 2.10. The crawl space is ventilated by outdoor air.
Figure 2.10: Illustration of the investigated construction in ”Moisture related problems in highly insulated
constructions (literature studies, measurements and calculations)” [39]. Only one part of the construction
has 50 mm insulation below the suspended floor.
Measurements were made in the wooden beams in both parts of the crawl space, illustrated as
M1 to M4 in the figure. A plot of the temperature versus moisture content below the beam is
made to evaluate the mould risk, as can be seen in Figure 2.11, where M1 is without insulation
and M2 is with insulation on the bottom side of the suspended slab.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
15
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.11: Figure from ”Moisture related problems in highly insulated constructions (literature studies,
measurements and calculations)” [39]. The figure is translated from Danish. The figure shows climate
relations, temperature versus moisture content, in wood in the lower measuring points M1 and M2. M1
has no insulation beneath the suspended floor, and M2 has a layer of 50 mm mineral wool insulation.
It is found that the extra insulation of 50 mm on the bottom side of the suspended slab has a
significant positive influence on the risk of mould attacks, as can be seen in the figure. The limit
values for new mould infestations and reinfected wood is shown in the figure at a level of 20 MC%
and 15 MC% respectively. The 50 mm insulation ensures that the temperature on the bottom of
the suspended floor is increased by 3-5 degrees above the insulation. The wood in this part of the
construction has a moisture content below 15 MC% relative humidity at all times and is therefore
assessed in the paper to be a safe solution.
The paper concludes that well ventilated crawl space solutions with insulation thickness between
200-300 mm and a layer of inorganic insulation below the beams would ensure acceptable mois-
ture content in the construction, and conditions favourable for mould growth will be avoided
[39].
Furthermore, it is found in the study that increased insulation thicknesses result in a more slowly
drying of moisture in the constructions, which means that it is important to keep the construction
materials dry in the construction phase [39]. It was also found from the study that the design
and execution of the constructions are very important, to ensure the construction is functioning
as intended. For instance, air-tightness of the construction is extremely important to limit the
risk of mould growth.
The paper ”Moisture conditions and energy consumption in heated crawl spaces in cold climates”
from 2003 from Finland [40] investigated whether heating of the crawl space was a feasible al-
ternative for crawl space moisture control. The investigations were made with simulations. An
illustration of the simulated construction can be seen in Figure 2.12. The thickness of the insu-
lation in the suspended floor is shown to be 20 cm.
16
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:29==
Figure 2.12: Illustration of simulated cold crawl space construction in paper ”Moisture conditions and
energy consumption in heated crawl spaces in cold climates” [40].
It was shown that a thick layer of light weight expanded clay aggregate at the bottom of the
crawl space prevents mould growth without heating. Another good alternative to control mould
growth found in the study was to heat the crawl space and use a ground cover without thermal
insulation. Also it was found, that with a reasonable ground insulation and air change rate (with
an optimal range between 0.5 and 1 ach) the energy consumption of heating was low, around 1%
of the energy consumption of a typical residential building.
The relative humidity was significantly lower in heated outdoor air ventilated crawl spaces com-
pared to unheated ones. It was found in the study that doubling the air change rate increased
the energy consumption by 20-60%. The study concludes that this shows that excessively high
air change rates should be avoided for example in naturally ventilated crawl spaces.
The control of the heating was based on a relative humidity controller with a set point. It was
found that heating the crawl space prevented all mould growth at the lowest output rate of 2.5
W/m2. The paper concludes that a design guideline for cold climate crawl space designs could
be a heated output rate of >2.5 W/m2, a controller with a set point of 75% RH, and air change
rate of 0.5 m3/(h m2).
It was found in the paper ”Temperature and relative humidity measurements and data analysis
of five crawl spaces” from 2017 that there in all five traditional cold crawl spaces in southern
Finland were conditions that promoted mould growth. It was found that the closer the crawl
space conditions were to the outdoor air conditions, the less severe moisture related conditions
occurred, such as condensation risk hours [41]. The paper concludes, that crawl spaces should be
designed to minimize thermal lagging, keep vapour excess at a low level and allow temperature
to rise in accordance with outdoor air.
2.3.3
Ground cover and air change rate
In the paper ”Crawl space air change, heat and moisture behavior” from 2000, measurements of
air change rate, temperature and humidity were made in similar crawl spaces in Finland with
different ventilation conditions [42]. The crawl spaces were ventilated with outdoor air, some
naturally and some mechanically by either supply or exhaust fans. An illustration of the tested
crawl space can be seen in Figure 2.13. It was found that a higher air change rate led to higher
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
17
==PAGE:30==
moisture evaporation from the uncovered ground, which affected the humidity in the crawl space.
Furthermore, pressure conditions also affected the humidity notably. Supply ventilation led to
the lowest relative humidities, and extract ventilation resulted in an even higher relative humidity
than natural ventilation. Among other things, it was found that ground moisture evaporation
could be reduced by using a ground cover such as gravel, expanded polystyrene, or plastic sheet.
Figure 2.13: Illustration of investigated crawl space construction in paper ”Crawl space air change, heat
and moisture behavior” [42].
The paper ”Moisture conditions of outdoor air-ventilated crawl spaces in apartment buildings in a
cold climate” from 2000 [43] investigated the effects of air change and ground covers on crawl
space moisture balance in a cold climate, in Finnish apartment buildings. It should be noted that
the crawl spaces studied were relatively warm due to a rather high U-value of the base floors
(around 0.35 W/m2K). In the study, the air change rates used were 0-10 ach. The study found
that ventilation is always required to remove the moisture, unless ground moisture evaporation
is entirely prevented, in which case the crawl space can be left unventilated.
It was found that higher air change rates increased the moisture evaporation from uncovered
ground, but it still resulted in a lower relative humidity. It was found that in the summer, there
was no upper limit for the air change rate, since the higher the air change the lower the relative
humidity. In the winter, an optimum air change rate of 2-3 ach was found, since higher air change
rates cooled the crawl space and raised relative humidity. It was found that ground covers are
the most effective measures to reduce relative humidity in the crawl space, and ground covers
with thermal insulation appear to be more effective than covers without insulation.
In the paper ”On the crawl space moisture control in buildings” from 2003 from Finland [44] it
was studied how relative humidity can be reduced by optimal selection of ground covers and
air change rates in both a warm and a cold crawl space construction. The cold construction is
illustrated as in Figure 2.12 and the warm construction is illustrated as in Figure 2.13. It was
found through simulations that a PVC cover can reduce the relative humidity in a warm crawl
space, but in a cold crawl space it does not provide any benefit in the summer, when outdoor
air is the main moisture source due to its negligible thermal resistance. In the summer, the cold
crawl space with PVC cover was as moist as the uncovered ground, and both cases produced
unacceptable conditions. Ground covers with a moisture capacity showed significant moisture
uptake in the summer, which decreased relative humidity in the crawl space. It was found that
a cold crawl space needs a two-speed air change; 0.5-1.0 ach in the winter and 2.0-5.0 ach in the
18
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:31==
summer. In relatively warm crawl spaces any ground cover and air change rate between 0.5-
2.0 ach give acceptable conditions. The weather data used, played a significant role, since the
Finnish test-year for energy calculations has a relative humidity of the outdoor air that already
caused mould growth on wood. Therefore high relative humidity in crawl spaces ventilated with
outside air could not be avoided.
In the paper ”Radon, fungal spores and MVOCs reduction in crawl space house: A case study and
crawl space development by hygrothermal modelling” mentioned in section 2.3.1, simulations of
different air change rates and ground coverings were made of the new crawl space [37]. It was
found through simulations, that an air change rate of over 2 ach caused too much cooling of the
crawl space in the winter and was not economically feasible.
2.4
Summary of literature review and historical overview
Traditionally crawl spaces have been used widely, especially in Scandinavia and other European
countries, and have historically been a very safe construction regarding moisture issues. The
traditional crawl spaces were reliant on the poor insulation between the occupied rooms above
to be moisture safe, since warmer temperatures in the crawl spaces have resulted in lower relative
humidities than what provides mould growth conditions. With the demands for better insulation
due to energy savings, the well-functioning of the crawl space disappeared.
Several studies and literature reviews concerning crawl spaces have been conducted through the
recent years to find alternative ways to construct crawl spaces more safely. The studies have
shown that relative humidity in crawl spaces can be reduced if:
• The crawl space conditions are similar to the outdoor environment. The risk of moisture
related issues is minimal.
• A ground cover is used in the crawl space. The relative humidity is reduced further, if a
ground cover with thermal insulation is used, since this increases the temperature in the
crawl space. It is found that a PVC membrane can not reduce the relative humidity in a
cold crawl space in the summer, since the main moisture source is from the outdoor air.
• The crawl space is heated along with using a ground cover with thermal resistance. This
results in additional energy consumption, however it was found to be a small contribution.
• A dehumidifier is placed in the crawl space.
• The crawl space is constructed as an indoor air ventilated crawl spaces. However, the
ventilation with indoor air can bring moist air down into the crawl space, leading to high
biological concentrations. The amount of material use for the construction is also high.
• The air change rate is increased. This is valid even though this also causes the ground
evaporation to increase. It was found in a study that too excessive ventilation is not fea-
sible, since it leads to cooling, which increases the energy consumption. However the
suspended floor in the study does not have an insulation layer, see Figure 2.8.
• Mechanical supply ventilation is used. Relative humidity increases, if mechanical exhaust
ventilation is used.
Furthermore, it was found that a 50 mm insulation layer below the wind barrier in the suspended
floor can have a significant positive impact on the moisture conditions in the beams for outdoor
ventilated crawl space [39].
Some of the disadvantages for use of heater, dehumidifier and mechanical ventilation in the crawl
space is that the solutions have to be maintained and should run all times in order to assure the
moisture safety of the crawl space.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
19
==PAGE:32==
The recommendations in the SBi guidelines align well with the findings in the literature study.
It is recommended to construct warm or highly ventilated crawl spaces in the SBi guidelines,
which is also found in the literature study to result in acceptable levels of relative humidity.
The material use of warm crawl spaces is quite significant, and therefore this solution is not
investigated further in this thesis. A ground cover is recommended in SBi guidelines as well,
which is found in the studies to be reducing the relative humidity as well.

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

3
Theory
3.1
Moisture theory
The relevant moisture theory is shortly summarized in this section, mostly based on SBi Guide-
line 277 ”Moisture in buildings - theory, calculation, and investigation” [45], if another source is
not mentioned.
3.1.1
Saturation point and relative humidity
Air can absorb and release moisture. Moist air is a mixture of dry air and water vapour. Air
can contain a limited amount of water depending on the temperature. The vapour pressure of
water can approximately be calculated as shown in Equation 3.1 and Equation 3.2, which are
determined empirically.
𝑝𝑚= 610.5 ⋅exp (17.269 ⋅𝜃
237.3 + 𝜃) for 𝜃≥0∘C
(3.1)
𝑝𝑚= 610.5 ⋅exp (21.875 ⋅𝜃
265.5 + 𝜃) for 𝜃≤0∘C
(3.2)
where 𝑝𝑚is vapour pressure of water at saturation [Pa] and 𝜃is the temperature in ∘C.
The relationship between water vapour content, 𝜈[kg/m3], and partial pressure can be obtained
as a function of the partial pressure and temperature, as shown in Equation 3.3.

The water content in the air is described as the relative humidity, 𝑅𝐻[%], which is the ratio of
the actual amount of water vapour, 𝜈𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑢𝑎𝑙, to the maximum possible amount of water vapour,
which is at saturation, 𝜈𝑚, as shown in Equation 3.4.

𝜈𝑚
(3.4)
Relative humidity depends on the air temperature as shown in Equation 3.1 to Equation 3.4; as
temperature increases, air can hold more water vapour. The maximum water vapour content in
fully saturated air is known as the saturation point. When saturated air is warmed, its relative
humidity decreases, allowing it to absorb more moisture. On the other hand, if saturated air is
cooled below the dew point, water vapour condenses into liquid. The dew point is the temper-
ature at which air becomes fully saturated with water vapour, leading to condensation. These
relations between temperature [∘C], relative humidity [%] and water content in air [g/m3] can
be seen in Figure 3.1.

in air. Saturated air is the maximum content of water in air at relative humidity of 100%. The illustration
is from SBi Guideline 277 [45], and translated from Danish.
3.1.2
Moisture in building materials
Most materials are porous, which means that they can absorb moisture, when in contact with
water or moisture. Materials can come in contact with moisture either in the production of the
material, through the surrounding air, from adjacent materials or by water from precipitation or
water damages. The moisture content is dependent on the type of material.
Materials that can bind moisture are called hygroscopic materials. These materials can exchange
moisture with the air. Hygroscopic materials can absorb or desorb moisture until they are in
equilibrium with the surrounding air moisture content, if the conditions of the air are constant
for long enough. This is called the equilibrium moisture content (EMC), which is in [MC%].
The equilibrium depends on the material type, for instance wood can contain more water than
concrete at the same relative humidity. This can be seen in the sorption curves in Figure 3.2.
The figure shows the water content in equilibrium with air for different materials at a constant
temperature.
22
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:35==
Figure 3.2: The figure shows sorption isotherms for different materials. The sorption isotherm for wood is
higher than concrete for instance, meaning that wood can contain more water than concrete at the same
level of relative humidity. The illustration is from SBi Guideline 277 [45], and translated from Danish.
The slope of the sorption curve shows the moisture capacity, which is most significant at either
high or low values of relative humidity. The material will here absorb or desorb larger amounts
of moisture at smaller changes in relative humidity. When comparing the materials in Figure 3.2,
it can be seen that wood have higher moisture capacity than concrete, and therefore absorb or
desorb larger amounts of moisture for the same change in relative humidity.
If the temperature is changed, the sorption curve will be higher or lower respectively, this is
shown for wood in Figure 3.3, which is from “Thermal/moisture-related stresses and fracture be-
havior in solid wood members during forced drying: Modelling and experimental study” [46].
