3.1.3
Moisture transport
Moisture can be transported either in liquid state or in vapour state. Transport in liquid and
vapour state can happen simultaneously in different directions. The most typical ways of mois-
ture transport in the building sector is diffusion, convection and capillary transport.
Diffusion is caused by movement of molecules, and is driven by differences in vapour pressure.
The water molecyles will move from regions of high vapour pressure to regions of low vapour
pressure.
Convection is caused by airflow and is driven by pressure differences. The pressure difference
can be caused by ventilation, wind pressure or temperature differences. Convection can cause
pressure differences over a construction, and if this happens, a transport of air and moisture can
happen through holes or cracks in the construction.
Capillary suction is transport in liquid form, where the water will seek towards the side with
lowest pressure. Porous materials can absorb water by capillary suction.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
25
==PAGE:38==
3.1.4
Vapour barrier
When constructions consist of multiple layers there is a risk of water vapour penetrating to
colder parts of the construction. If the water vapour content is higher than the saturation point,
water can condensate on interior surfaces in the construction. This humidification can impose
a risk of mould and other damages, see more in section 3.2. It should be ensured that air can
pass through the construction without humidification happening. A water vapour barrier on
the inside of the construction can limit the amount of moisture entering the colder sides of the
construction through diffusion.
3.1.5
Moisture calculations
Moisture calculations can be used to evaluate the well functioning of envelopes exposed to ex-
terior and interior environmental loads. Moisture calculations can be performed using both
stationary and instationary methods. Stationary methods, such as the dew point method and
Glaser’s method, are relatively simple and assume constant conditions on both sides of the con-
structions. These methods also assume that vapour moves in only one direction.
More detailed calculations can be made in hygrothermal programs, such as BSim, MATCH or
WUFI®. WUFI® will be used in this thesis, and further detail about simulations in the program
can be seen in section 3.3. The standard DS/EN 15026:2023 set requirements for transient hy-
grothermal simulation tools [48].
Quite a few publicized articles contain measurements as well as simulations of conditions in
crawl spaces. However, there is not yet publicized calculations to determine the conditions in
crawl spaces. For instance, Danish Technical Institute (DTI) has developed an internal spread
sheet for calculating the conditions in a crawl space depended on varying conditions, used in
”Improvement of energy and moisture conditions in crawl spaces” [49]. However, this spread sheet
is not optimal for predicting the conditions in a new crawl space construction, since the data has
to be calibrated with measurements in the particular crawl space.
Important parameters to do calculations are exterior and interior climatic conditions (tempera-
ture and relative humidity), and material parameters. Moisture calculations are uncertain since
the input data can be uncertain or unknown. The outside conditions vary significantly due to
the weather.
3.1.5.1
Surface transfer coefficients
At both inside and outside surfaces of constructions, there is a small difference in temperature,
due to the thermal contact between the surface and the air. Surface transfer coefficients are
therefore introduced, and quantifies how effectively the heat is transferred from the surface to
the air. The surface transfer coefficients can be found from the standard DS 418:2011 + Till. 1:2020
”Calculation of heat loss from buildings” [50]. For vertical downwards direction of heat flow, the
surface transfer coefficient for inside surfaces is determined to 𝑅𝑠𝑖= 0.17 m2K/W and outside
surface to 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.04 m2K/W. Outside surfaces have more extreme conditions, for instance wind,
which increases the heat transfer, and results in a lower surface transfer coefficient.
Surface transfer coefficients specifically relevant for crawl spaces are not known. When the
air change rate is relatively low, as it is expected to be in traditional crawl spaces (found to be
between 0.33 and 1.05 ach for three crawl spaces in ”Temporary mould remediation of crawl spaces”
[51]), it can roughly correspond to the indoor air exchange rate, at around 0.5 ach. The surface
transfer coefficient could then be 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.17 m2K/W. For more ventilated crawl spaces, the
surface transfer coefficient can be assumed to be lower, as low as 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.04 m2K/W as outdoor
conditions.
3.1.5.2
Outdoor climate and soil temperature
The humidity of the air varies a lot during the day due to temperature fluctuations, and variations
are significant over the year. When the temperature drops in the night, the relative humidity
26
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:39==
rises, perhaps even up to 100 %, where dew drops and fog can be formed. In the summer, the
average water content is around 10 g per. m3 leading to a relative humidity of around 75 % for
at temperature of 17 ∘C, as can be seen in Figure 3.1. In the winter the average water content is
5 g per m3 leading to a relative humidity around 90 % for the outdoor air at 3 ∘C. The relative
humidity during the year can be seen in Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: Illustration of relative humidity and moisture content in the air through the year. Illustration
from SBi Guideline 277 [45], translated from Danish.
The soil temperature varies over the year as well. Figure 3.6 shows the variation for different
depths in the soil.
Figure 3.6: Illustration of temperature in soil over the year at different depths. The illustration is from SBi
Guideline 277 [45], translated from Danish. For crawl spaces, the depth 0.6 m is relevant, however the line
is missing from the figure. The line can be found in SBi Guideline 178 [27].
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
27
==PAGE:40==
Climate changes
Climate change and global warming happens due to higher levels of CO2 and other green house
gasses in the atmosphere [52]. The global warming can lead to a future increased moisture load.
Due to the rising temperatures the atmosphere will be able to hold more water vapour, which
can lead to more frequent and intense precipitation events. It has been found by DMI that a
temperature rise of 1.5 ∘C can lead to an increase in precipitation of 15 % [53].
A rise in sea levels has been detected as well, primarily caused by meltwater of land-based ice
sheets and thermal expansion of sea water, due to the rising temperatures [54]. A rise in pre-
cipitation and higher sea levels can lead to higher ground water levels as well [53], since the
soil becomes saturated with water after prolonged periods of heavy rainfall [55]. In areas where
the groundwater level is high, increased rainfall will lead to more frequent and severe flooding.
This can possibly be mitigated by effective climate protection measures [56]. These increases in
moisture loads has to be considered, when designing crawl spaces.
3.2
Mould theory
Critical moisture conditions occur when moisture levels reaches a point that can lead to damages,
such as rot, wood-decaying fungi, or mould, which negatively impact the indoor climate. Mould
growth is the most moisture-sensitive of the possible damages, and therefore this is used at the
lower limit for acceptable moisture conditions [49]. Mould growth is a significant health risk,
since mould spores are known to cause various illnesses in humans. The mould criteria is 𝑅𝐻≥
75 %, which means that if the relative humidity does not exceed this value, there is no risk of
mould growth [45]. This level corresponds to a moisture content in wood of about 16-18 MC%,
as can be seen in the sorption curve for wood in Figure 3.2.
A prerequisite for mould growth is nutrition, moisture and a suitable temperature. Nutrition
can be accumulation of dust in older buildings or from organic structures and materials. The
most optimum temperatures for mould growth are between 20-30 ∘C, but temperatures between
5-40∘C can promote mould growth [49]. It can take time for mould growth to occur even when
all prerequisites are present. The germination time on organic material is more than 16 days, at
a constant level of 𝑅𝐻= 75 % [57]. The spores can survive at much higher temperatures as well
as at temperatures below the freezing point, allowing them to begin germinating again when the
right temperature and relative humidity are present [58].
The critical moisture content for wood is 20 MC% at novel infections (in equilibrium with air
around 87 %) and 15 MC% in wood that has previously been infected (in equilibrium with air
around 73 %) [45].
The most efficient way to ensure that mould growth does not occur, is to ensure that the moisture
conditions at the surface are below the critical level. Mould infections are difficult to predict, since
it is dependent on a lot of factors. For assessment of mould risk used in this thesis see section
3.3.1.2.
Mould growth can be investigated in multiple ways, including agar mould samples, DNA testing,
tape lifts, and more. In this thesis, agar mould samples were chosen based on guidance from
DTI. The samples are pressed against the surface to be assessed. Afterwards, the samples are
cultivated, and after 5-7 days they can be evaluated.
3.3
Hygrothermal simulations
Hygrothermal simulations are performed in this thesis by the use of WUFI® Pro 6.8. The calcula-
tion model, which the original version of the WUFI® program is based on, is described in detail in
the paper ”Simultaneous Heat and Moisture Transport in Building Components” [59]. The program
28
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:41==
is further developed and supported by Fraunhofer Institute for Building Physics (Fraunhofer IBP)
[60].
WUFI® stands for Wärme- und Feuchtetransport instationär, and translates to ”heat and mois-
ture transport”. The program is able to calculate the transient coupled heat- and moisture be-
havior of multi-layered building components under climatic boundary conditions, in one- and
two-dimensions [59, 60]. The program calculates transport and storage functions largely derived
from standard material parameters [59]. It is a non-linear equation system, and can therefore only
be solved numerically. WUFI® has numerous times been validated with experimental data and
standards [61, 62, 63].
The model takes into account the main hygrothermal effects [62, 61], such as
• moisture and heat sources and sinks inside a room and inside a component
• moisture input from the envelope due to capillary action
• heat input from the envelope
• diffusion and vapour ab- and desorption as a response to the exterior and interior climate
conditions
• solar energy input through walls and windows
• hygrothermal sources and sinks due to natural or mechanical ventilation
3.3.1
Use of simulation program
In the flow chart in Figure 3.7, from “Hygrothermal behavior and simulation in buildings” [64],
the input, output and post-processing for hygrothermal calculations in WUFI® can be seen. In
section 3.3.1.1 input parameters are described, and in section 3.3.1.2 output and post-processing
model are described.
Figure 3.7: Illustration of flowchart for the use of WUFI®, from “Hygrothermal behavior and simulation in
buildings” [64]. The flowchart includes input, output and post-processing for hygrothermal calculations.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
29
==PAGE:42==
3.3.1.1
Input
The input for the simulation is the geometry of the building component, material parameters,
boundary conditions, initial conditions and numerical parameters. The construction is described
by layers of materials with assigned thicknesses chosen from the WUFI® material database. The
necessary material properties are among others density, specific heat capacity, thermal conduc-
tivity, and porosity.
The necessary boundary conditions for the exterior climate are the air temperature and relative
humidity, short and long wave radiation, precipitation, wind speed and direction. For crawl
space constructions, only relative humidity and temperature are relevant, due to the construction
facing ground. For the interior climate, the boundary conditions are the interior air temperature
and relative humidity [48]. The standard DS/EN 15026:2023 states that measured data can be
necessary to define the outside boundary conditions for components adjacent to crawl spaces
[48].
The temperature distribution over the building component normally adapts very quickly to the
boundary conditions, therefore a uniform temperature close to the expected mean can be se-
lected as start value. Moisture transport processes are, however, comparatively slow. Therefore
a realistic initial distribution may be important for the outcome of the simulation. It can be ben-
eficial to make the simulation over a few years, so that building components containing some
initial moisture can dry out. As initial conditions, each layer can be described by a specific water
content. It is possible to assign typical built-in moisture.
3.3.1.2
Output and post-processing models
The output of the calculation is the temperature, water content and relative humidity distribu-
tions through the building component. They may be presented as profiles for specific points in
time or as temporal variations at a specific location within the building component. Furthermore,
isopleths can be plotted, which combines relative humidity and temperature. These isopleths are
used as input data in post-processing models, which can help predict mould growth.
Among others, two models for numerical prediction of mould growth have been developed;
WUFI® Bio developed by Fraunhofer IBP [65] and the Mould Index model developed by Tech-
nical Research Centre of Finland (VTT) [66, 67, 68]. WUFI® Bio is based on comparison of the
calculated transient boundary conditions with the growth conditions for typical moulds found
on building materials [65]. WUFI® Bio is only suitable for interior surfaces, and is therefore not
applicable for crawl spaces when investigating the outside of the construction. Therefore, the
model will not be further elaborated.
The Mould Index model developed by VTT is an empirical model based on visual findings of
mould growth, using a six-point scale to describe the intensity of growth on the surface. A con-
version function between the two models have been developed [68], and the Mould Index model
has been included in WUFI® as an add-on, developed in collaboration between VTT and Fraun-
hofer IBP [69]. The model calculates the development of mould growth on a surface exposed
to fluctuating temperature and humidity conditions. The model includes the effect of exposure
time, temperature, relative humidity, and dry periods. The Mould Index model is experimentally
validated [67].
The original mathematical model describes the response of pure wooden material to arbitrary
temperature and humidity conditions [66], and has since been expanded to include a broader
range of materials and a variety of conditions [67, 68]. The mould index follows integer values,
as shown in Figure 3.8. Critical conditions for the initiation of mould growth is 𝑀= 1, which
means that just some growth can be detected microscopically no matter how much time passes
[66]. All values below 1 indicate no growth. For a mould index at 𝑀= 6 the mould will eventually
cover the whole surface regardless of the temperature.

Inputs for the mould index calculation are the sensitivity class as well as the material class, type
of surface, and type of wood. For material class the options are; ”Significant decline”, ”Relevant
decline”, ”Relatively low decline”, and ”Almost no decline”. The decline describe the rate at which
mould infestation decreases when conditions are no longer favourable for mould growth. For
type of surface the choice is between ”Planed” or ”Rough”. For type of wood the choice is between
”Softwood” or ”Hardwood”. Furthermore, an occupant exposition class has to be chosen, which
could either be ”Indoor surface or position in contact to indoor air”, ”Surfaces inside constructions
without direct contact to indoor air”, or ”No impact on occupants expected”.
The mould risk evaluation is assisted by a traffic light scheme, which indicates uncritical condi-
tions with green and clearly critical ones with red based on the occupant exposition class [69].
For yellow results, an assessment is necessary. The overview of the assessment of the traffic
light evaluation is shown in Figure 3.12.

clearly safe conditions at the assessed point, whereas red shows critical conditions. At yellow results
an assessment has to be made. The figure is from ”Guideline for assessing the risk of mould growth with
WUFI®” [71].

4
Method
This method section includes explanations of investigations of existing constructions towards
terrain, with a focus on modular constructions. The analysis is based on interviews with vari-
ous experts in the construction sector, and efforts have been made to collect measurements and
other investigations of the constructions towards terrain. Additionally, registration reports from
Byggeskadefonden have been reviewed to gain further insights.
Furthermore, one-dimensional hygrothermal simulations are made, to investigate the response
of the crawl space and organic ground slab constructions to realistic moisture loads. The use
of the simulation program is calibrated. Finally, an LCA screening has been made, to assess the
environmental impacts of four different potential structures.
4.1
Danish cases of constructions towards terrain
4.1.1
Byggeskadefonden
Byggeskadefonden (Danish for: Building Damage Fund) is a Danish organization that inspects
new and renovated publicly supported housing, and covers costs of building damages if they
should appear. Byggeskadefonden conducts 1- and 5-year inspections of the buildings, to ensure
quality and identify potential issues. The inspections check if the buildings have been designed
and executed in accordance with good building practice and current regulations. The purpose
of the 5-year inspection is to check whether the defects and damages recorded during the 1-year
inspection have been remedied.
Consultants, suppliers, and contractors who design social housing projects must submit a Decla-
ration of Risk-prone Conditions (In Danish: Erklæring om risikobehæftede forhold) to the client.
This should be done twice; at the preliminary design and at the detail design, regardless of
whether there are risk-prone conditions or not. The declarations are intended to prevent projects
from containing constructions and details where the designers should be able to foresee that sig-
nificant defects and errors are likely to occur during construction [72].
When investigating the use of crawl space and organic floor slab constructions in recent years,
the inspection database from Byggeskadefonden have been used to gather data [72]. Peder Fyn-
holm, technical director at Byggeskadefonden, have provided a list of relevant buildings con-
structed by Scandi Byg and BM Byggeindustri. The list can be seen in Appendix A. This list
provides the registration number for the relevant buildings in the database.
Furthermore, a goal for Byggeskadefonden is to compile and share experiences to improve con-
struction quality and prevent future building damages. Byggeskadefonden publishes guides and
articles on best practices and common issues. Furthermore, experience sharing is facilitated
through for instance a project day ”Byggeskadedagen”, which was held by IDA Byg on Novem-
ber 19th 2024 [73]. People from different parts of the building sector gathered and shared experi-
ences and discussed the green transition of the construction sector among other things. A recap
of discussed issues relevant to this thesis is included in section 5.2.
4.1.2
Interviews
Further information regarding the present use of constructions towards terrain, is gathered
through interviews with present and previous employees at contractor companies, administrator
companies, and Byggeskadefonden. The interviewees were asked about their knowledge about
and experience with the constructions, and their perceptions of the required common technical
knowledge and Byggeskadefonden’s expectations, among other topics.
34
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:47==
In the case of contractors, Horst Günther, previous technical director at Scandi Byg, and Chris-
tian Madsen, technical director at BM Byggeindustri, have been interviewed. Regarding admin-
istrators at housing associations Jesper Birn Lindeberg from KAB and Morten Lundsteen from
Lejerbo has been interviewed. Dorthe Johansen from Byggeskadefonden has provided clarifica-
tion on some of their perspectives.
Horst Günther is at present moment head of competence for timber construction at Sweco Dan-
mark, but was formerly technical director in Scandi Byg for around 15 years until 2023. He was
among other areas responsible for building physics. The interview with Günther was conducted
the 27th of November 2024.
Christian Madsen is technical director at BM Byggeindustri. In his department they advise de-
velopers and consulting engineers, and create detail designs for production. The interview with
Madsen was conducted the 13th of December 2024.
Dorthe Johansen is technical project manager at Byggeskadefonden, and made a presentation on
damages in buildings at the project day, Byggeskadedagen. The interview with Johansen was
conducted the 9th of January 2025.
Jesper Birn Lindeberg is project manager at KAB and has been with the company for 15 years.
He has been a part of several AlmenBolig+ projects from design to operation, including Folden,
Danmarkshusene, Irishaven, and Alberts Have. The interview with Lindeberg was conducted
the 10th of January 2025.
Morten Lundsteen works as operations manager at Lejerbo, in the region South of Køge. He is
responsible for about 2000 housing units, where he is primarily focusing on operations, including
finances and board meetings, as well as building maintenance and personnel. The interview with
Lundsteen was conducted the 26th of November 2024.
4.1.3
Moisture measurements from constructed buildings
A part of the aim of this thesis has been to collect moisture measurements from crawl spaces
and organic ground slabs constructed in recent years, which comply with current insulation
requirements. This task has proven to be more challenging than initially anticipated.
There are two primary objectives for gathering measurements of moisture in the constructions.
Firstly, the measurements can demonstrate whether the solutions are moisture-safe. Secondly,
to perform the simulations desired in this thesis, they must be calibrated with real measurements
to ensure the simulations are realistic.
During the interviews conducted, the interviewees were asked, if they had access to recorded
measurements. In the cases of interviews with Horst Günther and Christian Madsen, they indi-
cated that moisture sensors are built into the ground slab or suspended floor. However, Günther
no longer has access to the data as he is not employed at Scandi Byg anymore. Consequently,
Scandi Byg was contacted to request the measured data. Unfortunately, they were initially not
able to locate the data, including both moisture measurements and air change rate measurements.
Bunch Bygningsfysik, who conducted the investigations in collaboration with Scandi Byg, was
also contacted. However, they do not have the data, as this is owned by Scandi Byg. Fortunately
at January 1st 2025 Camilla Günther Lydersen was re-hired at Scandi Byg, and she was able to
locate the measurements and kindly sent them to be included in this thesis.
In the case of BM Byggeindustri, they generally do not monitor the buildings themselves; this re-
sponsibility lies with the building owners or administrators. However, Madsen has kindly shared
measurements from the one building, that BM Byggeindustri has responsibility for monitoring.

4.2
Simulations
One-dimensional simulations are performed in WUFI® Pro 6.8 [75]. Prior to the use of the pro-
gram, the model is calibrated and the influence of key parameters, such as materials and surface
conditions, are tested, see section 4.2.1.
Constructions of the suspended floor and organic ground slab, inspired by drawings found through
Byggeskadefonden’s database, are simulated. The indoor and outdoor conditions and other pa-
rameters are estimated to be as realistic as possible for the current climate. The description of
the simulations can be found in section 4.2.2.
4.2.1
Calibration of use of the simulation program
To calibrate the use of WUFI®, the simulations are compared with accessible data. The measure-
ments utilized have been used in paper ”Moisture related problems in highly insulated construc-
tions (literature studies, measurements and calculations)” [39], and BUILD Aalborg University has
kindly provided the raw data and permitted it to be used for this thesis.
4.2.1.1
Description of construction used for the calibration
In a residential building in Denmark, temperature and wood moisture have been measured in two
levels inside the construction, as well as in the crawl space and indoor. The measuring points
within the construction are placed in two layers in two locations, as shown in Figure 4.4 with
M1 to M4.
Figure 4.4: Illustration of the suspended floor structure above the crawl space, from ”Moisture related
problems in highly insulated constructions (literature studies, measurements and calculations)” [39]. There
are two parts of the construction; (a) without insulation installed under the wooden beams, and (b) with 50
mm insulation installed under the wooden beams. Placement of measuring points within the construction
are shown with coloured points M1 to M4.
The construction consists of 200 mm wooden beams with flexible insulation in between. Above
the beams are 50 mm hard mineral wool, a 100 mm layer of concrete above a vapour barrier and
tiles as finish. Below the beams is a wind-barrier and wooden furring beneath. On one side of
the construction a layer of 50 mm mineral wool is placed beneath the construction, and on the
other side, the mineral wool is removed for the investigation. The height of the crawl space is 700
mm, and the ground below is covered with a 100 mm layer of gravel. The ventilation conditions
are unknown, but can be assumed to comply with current recommendations for traditional cold
38

==PAGE:51==
crawl spaces, with a ventilation area of 1/500 of the ground floor area, resulting in ventilation
rates in the 0.3-1.1 ach span. For the calibration, the measurements from the part of the building
with insulation beneath the suspended floor is used (sensors M2 and M4).
The construction is modeled as closely as possible to the description in the paper, with the ac-
cessible materials from the WUFI® database. The material parameters for the used materials can
be seen in Appendix C.1.1.
Two different constructions have been simulated; one with a wooden material in the full height
of the beam layer, as shown in Figure 4.5a, and one with a thin wooden layer at the top and
bottom of the layer, with insulation in between, as shown in Figure 4.5b. The latter construction
will be called the sandwich construction going forward.

(b) Sandwich construction. Construction with a layer of

Figure 4.5: Illustrations of constructions simulated in the calibration.
For the simulations with beam in the full height of the section, the construction in WUFI® is
similar to the sandwich construction, with two 10 mm layers of wood material in each end, to
ensure that differences in material boundaries do not affect the results. For each of these two
construction types, two simulations are made with two different wooden materials available in
the WUFI® database.
Wooden material
Two different spruce materials from the WUFI® database is used; Spruce (density: 600 kg/m3)
and Spruce, radial with a density of 455 kg/m3. To distinguish the two, they will be referred
to as Spruce 600 and Spruce 455 respectively. The sorption curves from the WUFI® database
are illustrated in Figure 4.6, together with the sorption curves from Equation 3.5 (general curve),
Equation 3.6 (desorption curve) and Equation 3.7 (adsorption curve). The moisture storage curves
from the WUFI® database are converted into curves showing wood moisture by the percentage
of water content relative to the density of the material.

tion 3.6), and isotherm for adsorption (Equation 3.7).
The moisture content at a specific relative humidity is different depending on which material is
used. For instance, the material Spruce 600 (dark purple curve) will evaluate a lower moisture
content than Spruce 455 (light purple curve) at the same relative humidity. The same is valid for
the hysteresis curves, where adsorption (blue curve) will evaluate a lower moisture content than
the desorption curve (green curve). The difference varies with different relative humidity.

The measurements provided by BUILD consists of temperature [∘C] and wood moisture [MC%]

The difference between the calculated relative humidity curves by the two equations, is higher
for the indoor relative humidity, at 8 %-points, than for the crawl space relative humidity, at
5 %-points. The cause for this is that at lower wood moisture levels, the curves are evaluated
differently. For instance, the wood moisture level of 10 MC% evaluate relative humidities around
56 % and 47 % for Equation 3.5 (general curve) and Equation 3.6 (desorption curve) respectively,
which can be seen in Figure 4.6, which is a difference of 9 %-points. A moisture level of 20 MC%
evaluates relative humidities of 89 % and 82 % respectively, which is a difference of 7%-points.
Climate conditions in WUFI® simulation
The data provided is gathered from 11th of April to 15th of November of 2015. The data does
not cover a whole year, which is necessary in WUFI®, to run the simulation for multiple years.
When the simulation is run for multiple years, the initial conditions’ influence die out, becoming
irrelevant. If the simulation is run for only the known period, it is very important to choose the
initial conditions very accurately, which is not realistic.
To be able to calculate for a whole year and make the calculations more simple, it has been
42
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:55==
chosen to describe the climate by sine curves, which are fitted to the measured data, as shown
in Figure 4.7 to Figure 4.10. The sine curves are evaluated to fit visually, and are also evaluated
quantitatively by root mean square error (RMSE), as is done in the paper ”Temperature and relative
humidity measurements and data analysis of five crawl spaces” [41]. The RMSE is calculated by
use of a macro in Excel, shown in Appendix D.1. The macro was developed with the assistance
of Microsoft Copilot. [76].
The parameters for the best fit are used as input in WUFI®. The parameters used to generate the
sine curves are listed in Table 4.1, as well as the RMSE.

Surface transfer coefficients
The indoor surface transfer coefficient is 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.17 m2K/W. The air change rate of the crawl
space in question is not known, but assumed to be complying with current recommendations.
Since this gives air change rates close to the indoor air change rate, a surface transfer coefficient
of 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.17 m2K/W is chosen.
Other choices
As initial conditions it is chosen to use typical built-in moisture for all materials, which can be
assigned in WUFI®. The simulation is run over 8 years to ensure that the influence of initial
conditions die out. The numerical grid chosen is Automatic (I), which makes the size of sections
the same in both ends of each material, see Figure 4.11.
Output from simulations
To evaluate the fit of the simulation to the measured data, the relevant calculated parameters
(relative humidity, wood moisture and temperature) are gathered from WUFI®Graph from the
simulated construction at key points. These key points are located in the top and bottom layers of
the beam layer, where data is gathered from the whole wooden layer, as illustrated in Figure 4.11.
Figure 4.11: Illustration of the sandwich construction. The moisture level is recorded at the wooden layers
showed with orange and outlined with a box.

==PAGE:56==
4.2.1.3
Performed tests in calibration and evaluation
The performed tests in the calibration are listed below. For some of the tests, it was found that
the tested parameter did not influence the results. In those cases, graphs are not included here,
but are found in Appendix C.1.2.
• Test of two different constructions, wood in the full section and the sandwich construction,
see section below.
• Test of wooden material, Spruce 455 and Spruce 600. See section below.
• Test of use of different sorption isotherms, tested with use of Equation 3.5 (general curve)
and Equation 3.6 (desorption curve). See section below. The equations are used both for
converting the measured wood moisture into relative humidity to use as input in the sim-
ulation and after the simulation for converting the results from relative humidity to wood
moisture.
• Test of surface transfer coefficients on the bottom of the construction. It is found that
it does not make a difference if the surface transfer coefficient is 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.04 m2K/W or
𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.17 m2K/W, which are evaluated to be the maximum and minimum possible values.
In Appendix C.1.2 the resulting graphs can be seen in Figure C.10.
• Test with a layer of 150 mm air beneath the bottom insulation layer in the suspended floor
construction. It is tested if this influences the transfer from the climate placed at the outer
surface of the layer of air to the bottom of the insulation layer. It is found that a layer of
air beneath the construction does not influence the results. In Appendix C.1.2, the tested
construction can be seen in Figure C.11, and the resulting graphs in Figure C.12.
• Test of wind barrier beneath construction. The wind barrier at the bottom of the suspended
floor has to be vapour permeable, i.e. the value of water vapour diffusion resistance factor,
𝜇, is low. It has been found that it does not influence the results, if the WUFI® material
Wood fibre board, hard - wind barrier with a water vapour diffusion resistance factor of 𝜇=
9 [-], or Asphalt Impregnated Paper (30 min Paper) with 𝜇= 472 [-] is used. Furthermore,
it has been tested by changing the water vapour diffusion resistance factor of Wood fibre
board, hard - wind barrier from 𝜇= 9 [-] to 𝜇= 0.001 [-], which also does not influence the
results. In Appendix C.1.2 the resulting graphs can be seen in Figure C.13.
The calibration is based on the evaluation of the moisture level in the bottom of the beam layer
(M2 in Figure 4.4), since this is the most critical parameter to estimate correctly, and also the
largest variations from measurement are found here, which can be seen later in this section. The
moisture is evaluated in two ways; by recording the wood moisture level directly from WUFI®
(shown with striped lines in Figure 4.13), and by calculating the moisture content from the rela-
tive humidity (shown with solid lines on the following graphs).
Test of different constructions
The difference between the two simulations with the full construction and the sandwich con-
struction, both with Spruce 455, can be seen in Figure 4.12. The simulated results are shown
together with the measurements of wood moisture.
44
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:57==
Figure 4.12: Test of the sandwich construction and the construction with the full beam layer of wood with
Spruce 455.
Both simulations result in higher wood moisture levels than what is measured. The sandwich
construction evaluates slightly higher moisture levels than the construction with wood in the
full section. The reason for this is that when the thermal resistance of the insulation layer is
not present, the temperature towards the outside of the structure, where the evaluation has been
made, will be higher, which results in lower relative humidity. The sandwich construction is
therefore closer to reality, due to including a more correct thermal resistance of the beam layer,
even though the deviation from measurements is higher.
The same tendencies are seen for Spruce 600 in Figure C.14 in Appendix C.1.2.
Test of different materials
Figure 4.13 shows the results from the simulations for the sandwich construction with the dif-
ferent materials, Spruce 455 and Spruce 600.
Figure 4.13: Test of the two materials; Spruce 455 and Spruce 600, in the sandwich construction. Striped
lines show wood moisture recorded from WUFI® and solid lines are calculated from relative humidity by
Equation 3.6 (desorption curve).
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
45
==PAGE:58==
It is shown in Figure 4.13 that there is a significant difference between the two wood materials
chosen when the evaluation is made with weight-% directly from WUFI® Graph (shown with
striped lines in the figure). This shows that the choice of material where the result is derived is
very important and can have significant influence on the results when evaluating wood moisture.
The two simulations more closely align when the moisture content is calculated by Equation 3.6
(desorption curve) from the relative humidity and temperature at the bottom of the beam layer.
However, there seems to be slight differences in both the amplitude and the point in time where
the maximum moisture level is measured, see Figure 4.13. Another reason for focusing on the
results calculated from relative humidity instead of moisture taken directly from WUFI®, is that
further simulations of other constructions are evaluated based on isopleths, where only relative
humidity and temperature are relevant.
Spruce 600 follows the curve of the measurements with a more uniform variation compared to
Spruce 455. However, the material Spruce 455 is more realistic than Spruce 600 since spruce, with
a strength of C24, is used for construction and has a density of 420 kg/m3 [77]. The largest
differences between the material parameters of the two chosen materials, Spruce 600 and Spruce
455 are density and porosity. It is not investigated further, if the largest contributing factor is
the density or the porosity, since the two parameters are linked. The material parameters can
be seen in Appendix C.1.1. To make the simulations more accurate, it should be known more
specifically which wooden material is used for the construction, to assess which material in the
WUFI® database fits better.
In Appendix C.1.2 in Figure C.15 the results from the construction with a full section of wood
using different materials can be seen, and they show the same tendencies.
Test of sorption isotherms
The results from the investigation of different isotherms for the sandwich construction with
Spruce 455 are shown in Figure 4.14, together with the measurements.
Figure 4.14: Test of the different isotherms for the sandwich construction with Spruce 455.
It can be seen in the figure that the choice of sorption isotherm has a small influence. It influences
the moisture load, since the relative humidity used for inside and outside conditions are evaluated
higher with Equation 3.5 (general curve) than Equation 3.6 (desorption curve), see Figure 4.7 and
Figure 4.9. When using the sorption curve Equation 3.5 (general curve), the results are closer to
the measured data than for Equation 3.6 (desorption curve). The difference is found to be 6 %.

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

In Appendix C.1.2 Figure C.16, the results from the investigation using Spruce 600 in the sandwich
construction can be seen, which show the same tendencies.

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
47
==PAGE:60==
The simulated moisture levels in Figure 4.16 are higher than the measured data for both top
and bottom of the construction. When the wood moisture is overestimated, it can lead to a
construction being assessed critically when evaluating the design, even though this is not the
case in reality. Continuing with the same composition and materials in further simulations, the
constructions are expected to be evaluated more critically than reality, which is on the safe side.
4.2.1.4
Uncertainties and errors
There can be various uncertainties in the simulations and measurements, which are estimated
below. First of all, simulating a structure in one dimension is a big simplification, therefore the
data is not expected to fit exactly with measurements.
The uncertainties for the measurements are estimated in the list below.
• Moisture measurement equipment: It is not known which measurement equipment was
used, and therefore it is not possible to state the specific uncertainty. For other moisture
measurement equipment, the uncertainty is in the ± 1-2% range for measurements in the
range of 8-30 MC% [78, 79]. The uncertainty is therefore assumed to be 2%.
• Conversion of measured resistance: The conversion of the measured resistance to mois-
ture level is assumed to be 5% [80].
• Variation of material: The coefficient of variation in the material is normally in the 10-20%
range [80]. The uncertainty is assumed to be 15%.
The uncertainties in the simulations are estimated in the list below.
• Conversion from wood moisture to relative humidity: Converting wood moisture to rel-
ative humidity and vice versa introduces uncertainties, since the exact sorption isotherms
for the materials are not known. In the calibration, two quite different sorption isotherms
are used, Equation 3.5 (general curve) and Equation 3.6 (desorption curve). The average
variation between each point in the two simulations is calculated to be 6 %. Furthermore,
the sorption curves are dependent on hysteresis, which is not taken into account in WUFI®.
This uncertainty is estimated from the two sorption curves, Equation 3.6 (desorption) and
Equation 3.7 (adsorption), in the relevant range for the crawl space climate to be 4 %.
Additionally, relative humidity cannot be directly reflected in EMC, as it is assumed for
the conversion in these models. There will always be a discrepancy between the mois-
ture measurement and the relative humidity. Relative humidity will fluctuate more than
EMC, which adjusts to the relative humidity over a longer period. Furthermore, the mea-
surement of the wood moisture can also depend on the depth of the measurement in the
wood material. Therefore, it is introducing an uncertainty, by using the calculated relative
humidity from wood moisture as input parameters.
• Coefficient of variation for material: The coefficient of variation for the material is as-
sumed to be the same as for the measurements. The uncertainty is therefore assumed to
be 15 %.
• Use of sinusoidal curves: The use of sinusoidal curves as input parameters have been
found to vary from the measured data by 8 % for temperature and 2 % for relative humidity,
averaged for both Equation 3.5 (general curve) and Equation 3.6 (desorption curve). The
variations are calculated from Table 4.1.
• Material properties: It is only possible to choose predefined materials from the WUFI®
database, unless specific tests are made of materials. This limits the possible choices that
might not fit the actual construction. It is also assumed that the materials are uniform,
which is not the case for wood, however, in the WUFI® database it is possible to choose
48
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:61==
spruce in both radial, tangential, and longitudinal direction. Radial direction is chosen for
Spruce 455.
As these errors are independent, the propagation of uncertainties for measurements and simu-
lations are given in Equation 4.2 and Equation 4.3 respectively.
𝜎𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠𝑢𝑟𝑒𝑚𝑒𝑛𝑡𝑠= √(2%)2 + (5%)2 + (15%)2 ≃16%
(4.2)
𝜎𝑠𝑖𝑚𝑢𝑙𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛= √(6%)2 + (4%)2 + (15%)2 + (2%)2 + (8%)2 ≃19%
(4.3)
A plot including the uncertainties for both measurements and simulation for the sandwich con-
struction using Spruce 455 is shown in Figure 4.17. It is seen in the plot that when uncertainties
are taken into account, the measured and simulated data overlap at critical points, with higher
level of moisture content, from June to November.
Figure 4.17: Measured and simulated wood moisture at the bottom of the beam (M2), plotted together
with the uncertainties, which are assessed to be 19% for the simulation and 16% for the measurements.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

Indoor climate
For all simulations the indoor environment is chosen in WUFI® to be compliant with DS/EN
15026 [48], where normal moisture load +5% is chosen, which is the design case. The climate can
be seen in Figure 4.25.
Figure 4.25: Indoor climate compliant with DS/EN 15026 [48]. The design case is normal moisture load
+5%.

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:65==
Outdoor climate
For the crawl space climatic conditions, the outside of the construction is chosen to be as the
outdoors. This is due to the high ventilation rate in crawl spaces in modular construction, which
will be explained further in section 5.3.2.
The outdoor climate is selected to be Bremerhaven, which is located in Germany, close to Den-
mark and near the ocean. It is assessed in ”Climate data for moisture simulations: producing a
Danish moisture reference year and comparison with previously used reference year locations” that
the weather approximately represents the Danish weather [81], and that the mould risk for Bre-
merhaven corresponds better with the Danish conditions, than for Lund and Rostock, which are
other candidates. However, it seems that the Danish reference year have more severe temper-
atures and relative humidity. Another possibility was to use the Danish Design Reference Year
(DRY) weather data, however this was not possible due to failures in the Excel program provided
by Fraunhofer IBP to convert the data into a file type useful for WUFI®. The outdoor conditions
can be seen in Figure 4.26.
Figure 4.26: Outdoor climate, Bremerhaven Moisture Reference Year.
For the organic ground slab the exterior climate is represented by a relative humidity of 𝑅𝐻=
100% all year round. The temperature is included in the simulation as a sinusoidal curve, fitted
to the temperature of the soil in 0.6 m depth, see Figure 4.27, which is around the bottom of the
EPS layer. This gives the sine curve a mean value of 9 ∘C and an amplitude of 8 ∘C.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

Surface transfer coefficients
As the indoor surface transfer coefficient, the standard value of 𝑅𝑠𝑖= 0.17 m2K/W is used. See
section 3.1.5.1.
As the exterior surface transfer coefficient for the crawl space simulation, a coefficient of 𝑅𝑠𝑒=
0.04 m2K/W is used, since the conditions in the crawl space are as outdoor conditions due to
high ventilation rates.
As the exterior surface transfer coefficient for the ground slab, the coefficient is chosen to be
𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.0 m2K/W, since the bottom layer in the construction is in direct contact with the soil.
Other parameters
As initial conditions, it is chosen to use typical built-in moisture for all materials, which can be
assigned in WUFI®. The simulation is run over 8 years to ensure that the influence of the initial
conditions die out. The numerical grid selected is Automatic (I), which ensures that the section
sizes are uniform at both ends of each material.

space the exterior surface is evaluated as well. For the organic ground slab the evaluation is only
made in the bottom Spruce 455 layer, since this is the only organic material towards the outside
conditions. See layer in illustration in Figure 4.28b. Any evaluation of layers on the warm side
of the constructions evaluate mould indices of 𝑀= 0.

4.3
LCA screening
The climate effects associated with constructing four different types of constructions towards
terrain are assessed, including;
• Traditional concrete ground slab
• Organic ground slab
• Crawl space with concrete pier foundation
• Crawl space with screw foundation
This is done to compare and test whether significant differences in emissions exists. This can be
done using the methodology Life Cycle Assessment (LCA), which is a standardized method used
to quantify a wide range of environmental impacts through all life cycle stages of a construc-
tion. The LCA conducted in this thesis is performed as a screening, focusing only on climate-
related impacts (emissions of CO2-equivalents). This is done following the requirements for
LCA-calculations as stated in Danish Building Regulations (BR18) [8]. BR18 utilize calculation-
principles from DS/EN 15978 ”Sustainability of construction works - Assessment of environmental
performance of buildings - Calculation method” [82]. The screening is conducted with the follow-
ing scope:
• Modules A1-A3, C3, C4 and D are included. These are emissions related to production
of raw materials, transportation of raw materials to factory and manufacturing (A1-A3),
waste processing (C3) and disposal (C4). D represents the potential benefits or effects
associated with the end of life. D is calculated but not included in the summed totals, as
required in BR18. Emissions related to; transportation of materials to the construction site
(A4), installation (A5), use phase (B1-7), demolition (C1) and transport to waste facility
(C2) are excluded.
• Emission-factors are taken from the updated generic dataset developed for the coming
revision of BR18 [83]. These are used as they represent sector and product averages mainly
from Denmark. The table is in Danish, and the Danish names of chosen materials can be
seen in Appendix E.1. Where generic emission-factors are not available, representative
Environmental Product Declarations (EPDs) are used instead, where GWP-total is used.
• The reference service life (RSL) of the included building materials is assumed to be equal
to the life time of the building, which is 50 years as stated in BR18.
• The declared unit is 1 m2 of construction towards terrain. Climate-emissions are presented
using the unit kg CO2-equivalents pr. m2 pr. year as required in BR18. The U-values
[W/m2K] for the constructions are calculated to 𝑈= 0.076 W/m2K for all constructions,
see Appendix E.2.
Material quantities for each assessed construction type is based on drawings from relevant build-
ings found in the database of Byggeskadefonden, see details in section 5.1.1. Quantities are ap-
proximations and based on estimated volumes of primary materials. Various small items such
as corner joints, screws and other fasteners are not included. The size of the assessed building
is 27 m x 11 m = 297 m2, based on approximate figures from the illustrations of foundations in
Appendix E.3 Figure E.5 and Figure E.6.
Emission-factors for L-elements, screw foundation and radon barrier are found in available prod-
uct specific EPDs; The L-element is from Gunnar Prefab AB published by International EPD Sys-
tem [84], the screw foundation is from BAYO.s published by EPD Danmark [85], and the radon
barrier from DAFA published by epd-norway [86].
56
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:69==
The screw foundation was chosen because it is the only screw foundation available in the EPD
Danmark database. The same applies to the L-element; it was the most representative EPD avail-
able, and no EPD was available for the Danish market.
The radon barrier is utilized for both the two ground slab constructions, serving as a radon
barrier, and also as the membrane beneath the layer of gravel in the crawl space. This choice is
due to the limited selection of vapour barrier EPDs available, and the barrier’s durability, which
is necessary for use in the crawl space.

For the BAYO.s screw foundation the mass factor (density) is assumed to be equal to that of
structural steel, since it was not declared in the EPD. The concrete is chosen to be a realistic
sufficient strength (C20/25), however the exposure class does not match constructions in terrain.
Nevertheless, it is assessed that this material is the best approximation.
For the L-element, the mass factor is also not provided in the EPD. Instead, the density is calcu-
lated from the weight per element and the dimensions given in the EPD. Each element weighs
1.9 tonnes. The dimensions are; width 3000 mm, height 900 mm, depth at bottom 1200 mm, and
thickness 125 mm. The density is calculated in Equation 4.4.
𝜌𝐿−𝑒𝑙𝑒𝑚𝑒𝑛𝑡=
1.9 tonne
3 m ⋅(0.9 m ⋅0.125 m + (1.2 m −0.125 m) ⋅0.125 m) = 2565.4 kg/m3
(4.4)
To find the emissions for the insulation materials, the R-value of the insulation must be multiplied
with the emisions, since the emission for each phase is declared per 𝑅= 1 m2K/W. The R-value
is calculated for each insulation material in the respective sections in section 4.3.1.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
57
==PAGE:70==
4.3.1
Estimation of material volume
For the four construction types, some components are assumed to be alike; wooden floor, chip-
board and vapour barrier. The varying factors are insulation, L-element, and foundation. For the
three others than the concrete ground slab, the wooden cassettes are similar. The quantities of
the materials that are alike, including the wooden cassettes, are shown in Table 4.4.
The I-beams in the cassettes are assumed to be spaced with 600 mm between, which means there
are 11 m/0.6 m = 18.3 ≃19 beams spaced on the width of the building. The beams are in the full
length of the building, and it is assumed that similar beams are in the perpendicular direction as
well, to close off the cassettes, the same number as number of strip foundations, see Figure 4.30.
The I-beams are calculated as a uniform material, consisting of construction timber. This is an
approximation since I-beam consist of construction timber in the flanges and high-quality OSB
(Oriented Strand Board) panels in the web [87], however this is equal for all three constructions,
and therefore does not significantly influence the results. There could be a slight variation in
the height of the I-beam between the constructions, however the effect on the calculation is
limited and therefore not taken into account. The cross sectional area of the 400 mm I-beams are
calculated in Equation 4.5.
𝐴𝐼−𝑏𝑒𝑎𝑚= 2 ⋅(45 mm)2 + 310 mm ⋅10 mm = 7150 mm2
(4.5)

4.3.1.4
Crawl space with screw foundation
The amounts for the crawl space with screw foundation is almost equal to the crawl space with
concrete pier foundation. The materials that are similar include the mineral wool layers, the
wind barrier, steel profiles, and the L-elements. The only difference is the foundation type, where
the number of screw foundations is assumed to be the same as pier foundations. According to
KRINNER, a producer of ground screws [88], the weight of one ground screw with a nominal
length of 2 meters is 29 kg. This weight is increased by 25% to account for uncertainties in
load-carrying capacities and depth of load-bearing layers in the soil. There is no reinforcement
required for this construction.

5
Results
This result section presents the findings from investigations of existing constructions, derived
from Byggeskadefonden’s 1- and 5-year registration reports, as well as interviews with experts.
Additionally, the section includes results from investigations of moisture and ventilation in ex-
isting constructions conducted by contractors. Furthermore, it also includes the results from the
hygrothermal simulations and LCA screening.
5.1
Danish cases of constructions towards terrain
The following list, shown in Table 5.1, contains an overview of KAB administered buildings
registered in Byggeskadefonden’s database [72], where the contractor is either BM Byggeindustri
or Scandi Byg. Included in the list is the year and location, as well as the chosen solution towards
terrain. Furthermore, it is noted in the table if there are comments made about the construction
during the 1- and 5-year inspection by Byggeskadefonden or in the declaration of risk-prone
conditions (Danish: Erklæring om risikobehæftede forhold).
The list is not exhaustive, as there are more modular buildings in AlmenBolig+ constructed by
other companies than Scandi Byg and BM Byggeindustri, as well as outside of AlmenBolig+
administered by other housing associations. The division Gartnergården has been included, even
though it is not part of AlmenBolig+, since it was found to be interesting, see note *8 in section
5.1.2.2.
The declaration of risk-prone conditions has been abbreviated as DRPC to simplify the table.
Byggeskadefoden has been abbriviated as BSF as well.

5.1.2
Notes from the registrations
In the following sections, notes regarding the concerns of the constructions towards terrain made
at the 1- and 5-year registration or in the declaration of risk-prone conditions are listed for each
division. Where moisture measurements are mentioned, the specific Danish words are included
to clearly present Byggeskadefonden’s expectation.
5.1.2.1
Notes for crawl space constructions
A declaration on risk-prone conditions has been presented to Byggeskadefonden, but is not at-
tached in the found documents for Enebærstien, Alberts Have, and Park Kvarteret, therefore it is
not known if the crawl space is included as a risk-prone construction. For Tømmergården, Nor-
dre Fælledkvarteret, and Skovkvarteret the crawl space is registered as a risk-prone construction
in the declaration.
Tømmergården, Skovkvarteret, and Nordre Fælled Kvarteret *1
The three declarations of risk-prone conditions for Tømmergården, Skovkvarteret, and Nordre
Fælled Kvarteret state that non-permanent measurements have been conducted in other modular
buildings constructed at the beginning of the AlmenBolig+ project since 2011, where ”no issues
were found in the form of increased relative humidity in the suspended floor construction”. It is
stated in the declaration, that it is advised (”anbefales”) to implement measurement points to
verify the relative humidity under the construction.
For Tømmergården it is written in the 1- year registration that ”Byggeskadefonden requires (”pålæg-
ger”) the building owner to increase the operation of the risk-prone construction by conducting reg-
ular readings (at least twice a year for the next five years) of the built-in moisture sensors.” For
Tømmergården there are built in moisture sensors, which have showed measurements of wood
moisture of 11.1-15.5 MC% during the 1-year registration. In the 5-year registration, nothing
concerning the crawl space is mentioned.
For Tømmergården and Skovkvarteret it is further mentioned in the 1-year registration that the
crawl space is even more risk-prone, since ”a mould-treated plywood board has been used as a base
for insulation. This does not follow the recommendations in the SBi Guideline 224, which prescribes
that a board of inorganic material should be used”. According to the description and drawings of
the constructions, the plywood board is covered by a 50 mm layer of insulation.
It is stated in the 1-year registration for Skovkvarteret that moisture sensors are placed under the
ground deck. Furthermore, it is written: Ongoing control via the built-in moisture sensors of the
organic plywood boards must (”skal”) be carried out, which should (”skal”) be incorporated into the
building owner’s operations and maintenance plan. The results of the ongoing moisture measure-
ments must (”skal”) be presented for Byggeskadefonden at the 5-year inspection of the building.”
In the 5-year registration for Skovkvarteret it is written that ”no drawings have been presented
showing where and how moisture sensors should be built into the constructions. During the 5-year
inspection, it was not possible to find components in the grilles around the apartment blocks that
would confirm that moisture sensors are built-in. The board of the division is completely unaware of
moisture sensors in the constructions, as stated by a board member [...]. The project manager from
KAB is also not aware of the issue with moisture sensors. Apparently, no form of control of moisture
measurements in the constructions has been carried out in the past 5-year period.”
Enebærstien *2
It is written in the 1-year registration for Enebærstien that Byggeskadefonden ”considers the
building part to be highly risk-prone”. It is noted that ”various moisture technical investigations,
calculations, and assessments of the current conditions have previously been carried out”. These
cannot be found in the database. It is further noted that some measures have been done to reduce
the risk, such as placing a plastic sheet over the terrain, designed with a 20 per mille slope from
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
69
==PAGE:82==
the center of the crawl space towards the facades, where perimeter drains have been established.
Furthermore, technical construction failure is registered concerning the corrosion category for
the steel plate that closes of the construction in the gables. See more in note *4.
Langhusene *3
Technical construction failure is registered for Langhusene concerning the corrosion category
for the steel plate that closes of the construction in the gables. See more in note *4.
Dortheavej *4
For Dortheavej it is noted that the steel plates in the gables are not tight, which means that the
protection of the edge beams is poor, and the effects of water ingress can impact the lifespan of
the load-bearing parts of the construction. This can be seen in Figure 5.1. Furthermore, there is
a note about concerns of joints at windows and doors being problematic, since it is assessed that
water can get into the construction if the joints or seals are performed badly and not tight.
Figure 5.1: A joint between steel sheets that is not sufficiently tight.
It is seen in the picture in Figure 5.1, that the joint between the steel sheets is not tight, and
water can penetrate directly to the construction, which is covered by a 12 mm facade board
(powerboard, see page 67). It is mentioned that the corrosion protection class is C3, which is not
sufficient when embedded in the ground, and this will lead to a decreased lifespan. A powerboard
is an MgO board that was widely used in the early 2010s, particularly as wind barriers in light
facade constructions. However, it was later discovered that these boards had a major drawback,
since they absorb moisture from the air, which could lead to corrosion of metal components and
rot or mould growth in wood. This issue resulted in one of the largest construction scandals in
recent times [89], but will not be covered further, as it is assumed that the use of these boards
has ceased.
It is noted that ”Byggeskadefonden considers it a failure, that the construction of organic materials is
located below ground level”. Furthermore, it is stated that ”inspection options must (”skal”) be built
in so that the suspended floor above the crawl space can be kept under observation”. The inspection
possibility could be implemented either by hatches in the floor or by installing moisture sensors,
which must be read at regular intervals.
It is stated in the registration, that several residents had closed the ventilation grilles with stor-
age, which reduced the ventilation area. This is a concern since the ventilation is crucial to the
moisture safety of the construction.
According to the 5-year registration, a report on the technical construction failure has been sub-
mitted by Scandi Byg. This report cannot be found in the material accessible at Byggeskade-
70
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:83==
fonden’s database. It is not mentioned in the 5-year registration whether moisture has been
measured at regular intervals.
Kløverbladsgade *5
In the 1-year registration for Kløverbladsgade it is written that ”the extent of ventilation for the
crawl space has not been described”. Additionally, it is noted in the 1-year registration that ”it
should be clarified how the treatment of plywood sheets with mould-killing agents on the plywood
is managed.”
Furthermore, moisture sensors have been installed under five houses, with two sensors per house.
One sensor is placed in the middle under the house, and one sensor is placed 1 meter from the
facade. Sensors with cables are used, which should be read manually. It is written that: ”A report
from the measurements with the moisture sensors must (”skal”) be presented, so that a complaint
letter regarding the matter can be sent to the relevant parties involved in the construction within 3
years from the completion of the building.”
In the 1-year registration a picture of the ventilation grilles was included, which can be seen in

==PAGE:84==
Gartnergården, see note *8. For all other constructions there are no notes of the construction in
the 1-year registration.
Asters Rækkerne, Linderækkerne and Store Solvænget *6
In all cases of Astersrækkerne, Linderækkerne and Store Solvænget, the same text was written
by Byggeskadefonden. It is noted that the cassettes are inaccessible for inspection, and therefore
that ”any attack by wood-destroying microorganisms can therefore become significant before the
attack is detected”.
It is written that ”Byggeskadefonden requires (”pålægger”) the building owner to conduct intensive
monitoring of the moisture conditions in the risk-prone construction towards terrain. The monitoring
must (”skal”) be initiated as soon as possible and continue for at least 3 years.”
At the 5-year inspection for Asters Rækkerne, it was registered that no damages, moisture, or
other issues related to the floors were found. It is stated that ”the situation is monitored and
addressed in connection with operation and maintenance”, however not how this is done.
At the 5-year inspection for Linderækkerne, it it stated that ”KAB has not conducted intensive
monitoring of the moisture conditions, such as by installing moisture and temperature measuring
devices. KAB states that they are in the process of preparing a tender for the clarification/remedia-
tion of the issue [...]. Remediation is expected to start mid-2024.” It has not been found what this
remediation is.
Skovhusene *7
The only note in the case of Skovhusene concerns deficiencies in the documentation material
regarding the type of insulation laid under the wooden modules in the ground floor slab, which
is not specified. The reason for the note is that insulation layer is expected to serve as the capillary
barrier, but it cannot be assessed if the material meets the requirements.
Gartnergården *8
In the case of Gartnergården, it has been found in one area, that the plinth trench was almost
completely filled with gravel. See pictures in Figure 5.3. This means that the bottom of the plinth
trench is significantly above the membrane on top of the insulation. It is written that ”the bottom
of the plinth trench must be at least 50 mm below the top of the concrete ground slab, which in this
case can be equated with the membrane on top of the insulation.”
72
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:85==
(a) Picture of plinth trench filled with
gravel.
(b) Picture of plinth trench filled with gravel. It is measured that the free
