Figure 5.3: Plinth trench at Gartnergården, which was completely filled with gravel at the 1-year regis-
tration. Pictures from Byggeskadefonden’s database [72].
Furthermore, it is stated in the 1-year registration that ”since the ground floor slab is ’pressed into
the terrain’ on top of a vapour barrier/radon protection on capillary-breaking insulation, it is crucial
that the radon barrier is tight [...]. This also applies around the installation of pipes, where the joints
between the pipes and radon barrier must be tight. Likewise, it is extremely important that the
membrane on the outside of the foundation and at the transitions between the foundation and the
wall is tight, and that the seals under doors and windows are tight, so that water does not penetrate
the ground floor slab construction from the plinth trenches.”
5.1.3
Summary of cases
From the analysis of the 1- and 5-year registrations by Byggeskadefonden it was found that the
buildings with the most detailed registrations performed, were also the ones where most issues
regarding the solution towards terrain was found. This can imply, that there might be issues
which are not covered concerning the solutions towards terrain.
One of the most frequently mentioned concerns about both constructions, is the lack of accessi-
bility for inspections, as no hatches have been installed. Byggeskadefonden states that moisture
sensors can be used to monitor the construction instead. It is required for several of the con-
structions to have built-in moisture sensors with monitoring for at least 3 years, however in
most of the buildings it seems that this is not complied with, or at least that the sensors are
not being monitored. For the division Skovkvarteret for instance, the administrators and the
project manager from KAB are unaware of this expectation, even though it is clearly stated in
the registration.
Byggeskadefonden is also concerned about seals and joints at windows or doors and protection
at the edge beams. This is written in registrations for both crawl space constructions and organic
ground slabs.
In general it is clear, that crawl space constructions used in AlmenBolig+ projects are considered
highly risk-prone by Byggeskadefonden. In the registrations it is only mentioned for Enebærstien
that the crawl space in itself is considered to be highly risk-prone and not in accordance with
the required common technical knowledge. This can possibly be explained by the highly ven-
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
73
==PAGE:86==
tilated crawl space first being included as an alternative to the traditional cold crawl space in
SBi Guideline 224 [90], which was published in 2013, whereas the building is from 2012. There-
fore the highly ventilated crawl space solution is accepted for the rest of the buildings. In the
registrations, the concerns of the crawl space are therefore often revolving around other factors.
It can be seen in virtually all cases, that a plywood board is used instead of an inorganic board
below the suspended floor, which is recommended in SBi Guideline 224 and 279. This plywood
board is covered by 50 mm insulation. Concerns about the use of steel sheets in the gables to
close of the construction are raised for a few buildings as well, being that the sheet is the only
separation between soil and organic materials in the construction. The corrosion class of these
sheets is not adequate, which reduces the life span and increases the risk for the construction.
Also, they are not always properly sealed, as was clearly the case for the division Dortheavej.
Furthermore, for the crawl space it is important that the residents do not close of the ventilation
grilles with storage.
In general it seems that the organic ground slab solution is more accepted by Byggeskadefonden
as there are not many comments in the 1-year registrations. Also, the construction is not regis-
tered as a risk-prone construction for most of the buildings. However, in some cases it is consid-
ered risk-prone, due to the cassettes being inaccessible. A few important issues are highlighted
for the organic ground slab in the registrations; it is important that the insulation below the cas-
settes can be used as capillary barrier, and that the radon barrier is tight, including around all
installations and joints. In one case, at the division Gartnergården, the plinth trench was filled
with gravel, which would damage the construction significantly, if not remedied.
74
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:87==
5.2
Interviews
The key insights from the interviews with Horst Günther, Morten Lundsteen, Christian Madsen,
Jesper Lindeberg, and Dorthe Johansen have been compiled and organized into the following
sections.
5.2.1
Thoughts of designs of constructions towards terrain
Originally Scandi Byg has built highly ventilated crawl space constructions, see section 5.2.4, in
their buildings but have in the later years transitioned to construct organic ground slabs instead.
Günther says, that the reason Scandi Byg transitioned, was highly influenced by Byggeskade-
fonden, since the two parties would always have a discussion regarding the solution towards
terrain. Günther says that Byggeskadefonden chose to state that a crawl space solution was a
risk-prone construction. Scandi Byg still had confidence in the crawl space solution but wanted
to avoid the inconvenience. Günther mentions, that he thinks that crawl spaces will be used
more going forward, since it can lower the CO2 emissions compared to the organic ground slab.
Günther emphasized that Scandi Byg has never experienced problems with crawl spaces in their
buildings. He said that some modular buildings around Denmark have experienced problems,
however it was, in his opinion, due to errors in craftmanship. For instance, if the sewer penetra-
tion was not constructed correctly. He says that it is very few buildings that have experienced
problems.
BM Byggeindustri have not used the ventilated crawl space solution since it is advised against in
the BYG ERFA experience leaflets. Madsen mentions, that he thinks the solution is risk-prone,
with reference to the leaflets. Madsen mentions, that the crawl space solution is more complex
than the organic ground slab solution, due to the inorganic board required underneath, and the
use of more insulation and the moisture exposure. The standard solution from BM Byggeindustri
is an unventilated organic ground slab, with traditional concrete strip foundation. The bottom
is polystyrene insulation which is covered by a radon barrier which also acts as the moisture
barrier. To ensure that there is no thermal bridge at the facades, foam glass insulation is used
above the foundation. Furthermore, insulation at the bottom of the slab adjacent to the facade is
not installed as showed in the drawing in section 5.1.1 on page 68, to keep up the temperature of
the corner area of the construction.
Regarding the organic ground slab, Günther mentions that the radon barrier is also a part of
the moisture barrier plan of the building, since it can be connected with the vapour barrier. In
some of the recent years at Scandi Byg, the installation of the radon barrier was included in the
production of the module, instead of being laid out at the building site. This limited the causes
of errors, since the barrier became very slippery at rainfalls at the site, which was a danger to
the workers, and also the radon barrier had to be dried before construction could continue.
Lindeberg notes that one advantage of crawl spaces as opposed to organic ground slabs is the
ability to detect water problems and address them in time, which can be more challenging, if not
impossible, with organic ground slabs.
BM Byggeindustri is in dialog with Byggeskadefonden throughout the process of the design and
construction of the buildings. Madsen says that BM Byggeindustri will not deliver a project
with risk-prone conditions. He mentions that the approach to meet this is to register the risk-
prone constructions to Byggeskadefonden early in the design phase, and then work on solutions
to mitigate the risks, for instance by using moisture sensors, and focus on documenting the
requirements for operation and maintenance properly. Another focus is ensuring the terrain
layout is properly designed.
Lindeberg says that during the design phase, KAB also engages in dialogue with Byggeskade-
fonden regarding construction principles.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
75
==PAGE:88==
5.2.2
Drainage of surrounding terrain
Madsen places high emphasis on the focus of evaluating and planning the surrounding terrain in
the design phase, to avoid moisture accumulation. BM Byggeindustri have a large focus on daily
drainage of the terrain and soils using primarily the gravitational principal. They make sure
that there are designs of storm water protection in the area with use of for instance Sustainable
Drainage Systems (SuDS), so that the water can be diverted to ditches. The risk of the area of the
building should be assessed in the design phase to determine whether the building is suitable to
build down into the ground. Furthermore, it is important to ensure that the SuDS have sufficient
capacity to prevent backflow. Madsen also says it is important to ensure that the areas where
the organic ground slab is used are not placed low in the terrain.
Lindeberg also emphasizes that the surrounding environment is a critical factor to assess and
examine. He suggests being more critical about the placement of buildings in the future, as it
requires significant preparation of the terrain to manage water. He mentions conditions such as
surface water drainage and ensuring that perimeter drains are functional and capable of directing
water away. Lindeberg says that there are high demands on the SuDS systems. If these systems
are not designed and executed correctly, it can pose a risk over time. He points out that residents
can unknowingly alter the terrain and disrupt the function of SuDS systems, such as by digging
a hole for a trampoline and relocating the soil elsewhere.
Lindeberg mentions, that the collaboration between advisors and contractors is not always opti-
mal. He notes that projects can change during construction, for instance by altering the slope of
the terrain, which can negatively impact water drainage. Additionally, during operation, solu-
tions for other problems may be implemented that unknowingly block water flow or infiltration,
such as by laying mats.
Günther says it is important to have a slope of 1:40 from the middle of the building to the facades
under the crawl space, so that the water can be led away.
5.2.3
Maintenance and operations
Madsen stresses the point of having a focus on the operation phase of the building. A risk during
the operation is the maintenance of the plinth trench. In projects where the principal of gravi-
tation cannot be used, drainage pumps are installed. Madsen says that it is very important that
the operators know that the pump cannot be taken out of service at any point, and that they
have to maintain the pump and ensure that it is running at all times. The whole safety of the
construction is dependent on the functioning of the pumps, and Madsen says it is problematic
that it is a possibility to turn of the pumps. This can be an issue in housing associations such as
AlmenBolig+ where the residents are in charge of the maintenance and operation. Madsen says
that BM Byggeindustri creates many operations and maintenance plans and stresses important
points, such as ensuring the maintenance of the pumps. However, Madsen sees a potential prob-
lem when the housing associations delegate the maintenance to the residents themselves and
does not ensure that the knowledge is transferred to them as well.
Lindeberg also finds it problematic to leave the maintenance of more complex constructions to
residents without technical insight. He also brings up the case of pumps and suggests imple-
menting a reporting system for pumps that report to a central system.
5.2.4
Prior investigations of constructions and accumulated experiences
Günther has in his job at Scandi Byg conducted many experiments regarding building physics,
some together with Bunch Bygningsfysik [91]. They have installed measurement equipment in
many places in constructions, to investigate how the components react to moisture loads. He
has conducted experiments with crawl spaces as well. The layout of the constructions produced
by Scandi Byg are developed based on the findings in these investigations.
76
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:89==
Günther mentions, that they have found that it is important to remember a layer of insulation be-
low the suspended floor construction. This is to ensure a higher temperature in the element and
moves the condensation point. Günther emphasizes, that this has a significant positive impact.
This knowledge is based on calculations, but he also mentions that due to the crawl space having
the same temperature as the outside, the suspended floor can draw parallels to an exterior wall.
A difference he mentions, between the exterior wall and suspended floor, is that there is a higher
moisture load from the ground, since the plastic sheet covering the ground is not completely
tight, and it is darker in the crawl space.
Günther mentioned a specific concern in the crawl space, which is at the connection to the strip
foundation. There has been a thermal bridge, which can be critical, since this can cause a low
temperature indoors at this spot, which can lead to mould growth. This have been dealt with by
removing some insulation in the floor at this location, in order to locally heat up the foundation.
This solution results in a slightly higher line loss, which is accepted to keep the construction
moisture-safe.
Günther is not concerned about the moisture safety of Scandi Byg’s crawl space solution, as long
as the sufficient quality control is made.
The ventilation is ensured by ventilation grills, as shown in Figure 5.2, in the whole periphery of
the building. In some cases, there were only grills at facades and not at the gables. Scandi Byg
have tested these ventilation solutions together with Bunch Bygningsfysik many times. The air
change rate is high all year round, and this is according to Günther the main reason Scandi Byg
have not experienced problems with the crawl spaces. The high air change rate ensures that the
conditions are as the outdoor conditions. The ventilation investigations made by Scandi Byg can
be seen in Appendix F. They are summarized in section 5.3.1.
5.2.5
Monitoring of constructions
According to Günther, Scandi Byg has monitored the buildings by continuous measurements
after the construction of the buildings. They have done this for many years. Scandi Byg has
gathered the measurements and monitored the buildings. The measurements, kindly provided
by Scandi Byg, are shown in section 5.3.1.
According to Madsen, BM Byggeindustri install sensors in virtually all their buildings, in all
locations they assess are risk-prone, when agreed with the owner. Especially the financially
supported buildings, such as AlmenBolig+, due to demands from Byggeskadefonden. However,
the buildings owners do not comission BM Byggeindustri to monitor the constructions, and they
are therefore responsible of monitoring the measurements themselves. Therefore, Madsen does
not have an overview of the measurements.
Furthermore, BM Byggeindustri has leak detectors in each module in the technical room. From
this leak detector, a sensor is connected to the bottom of the ground slab at the radon barrier.
With this installation, Madsen explains that if moisture accumulates at the membrane, it will
eventually be led to this moisture detector, alerting the building owner.
Lundsteen informs that Lejerbo does not monitor the organic ground slab construction, other
than visually from the outside. Lindeberg states that KAB has not installed moisture sensors in
the constructions and therefore has not conducted moisture investigations. Johansen mentions
that she recalls that in the beginning of the AlmenBolig+ projects, KAB conducted measurements
on multiple buildings. This is also written in the declaration of risk-prone conditions for some
of the crawl spaces, see note *1 in section 5.1.2.1.
Regarding Byggeskadefonden’s recommendations to install moisture sensors in buildings, Jo-
hansen stresses the importance of involving professionals, as administrators or building owners
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
77
==PAGE:90==
may not have the necessary capacity or skills. It is a possibility that the contractors can handle
the monitoring, however this is an expensive solution over a long period. Johansen says that,
given Byggeskadefonden’s current organization, it would not make sense for them to monitor
the buildings. This responsibility lies with the administrators of the housing association at the
moment.
5.2.6
Damages and concerns
Both Madsen and Günther says that they have not experienced moisture damages in the organic
ground slab or crawl space. Lundsteen also says that Lejerbo have not experienced problems
with the organic ground slab.
At the project day, Byggeskadedagen, Dorte Johansen talked about modular buildings. She noted
that Byggeskadefonden has ”loudly expressed concerns about modular constructions, but fortu-
nately, we have not been proven right yet”. She mentioned that not many damages have been
discovered so far.
Johansen however mentioned a specific case of a modular building that suffered significant mois-
ture damage in the organic ground slab. The building was constructed in 2019 on a marsh, and
experienced damage in 2022. Photos from the construction showed water at the foundations,
and the installed SuDS were insufficient to drain the water away. There was a discussion a the
project day about the importance of considering the building’s location in the risk declaration,
which was not done in this case.
At the interview conducted with Johansen, she expressed concerns about organic ground slabs
and crawl space constructions, as they are placed into the ground and built in a way that makes
inspection and monitoring difficult. She suggested that the apparent ”lack” of damages might
be because they are not actively looked for, unlike damages in masonry for instance. She says
that Byggeskadefonden worries that these damages might simply not have been discovered yet
and could emerge later. She emphasized the importance of carefully considering the building’s
location and noted the problems with buildings rely on pumps for moisture-safety.
Johansen also mentioned that climate change has led to changes in groundwater levels across
various regions of Denmark. Furthermore, there are changes in precipitation, and cloudburst
happen more frequently. The consequences of these changes for the constructions are unknown.
Even if a construction is designed correctly, the impact of changing groundwater levels is un-
certain. She says that these constructions lack resilience against unforeseen changes in moisture
loads, such as rising groundwater levels.
Another concern Johansen mentions is the connection details, e.g., at exterior doors, where pro-
tection against water ingress into organic materials relies on elastic joints and similar parts that
get worn down and require a high degree of maintenance. This is particularly a concern in
housing associations like AlmenBolig+, where residents handle the maintenance. Additionally,
hidden plumbing installations with limited lifespans will be difficult to replace and detect if they
are damaged and leaking.
Lindeberg suspects there may be a conflict of interest since Byggeskadefonden is also the insur-
ance company and have its own interests in minimizing payouts. He mentions, that he notes
a difference between requirements and recommendations in the registrations by Byggeskade-
fonden, and therefore he states that KAB does not expect that the lack of moisture measurements
will be a problem, if moisture damage occurs in the slabs, even without taking measurements.
Johansen on the other hand states that presenting measurements is required to receive compen-
sation.
78
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:91==

Investigations of moisture measurements and ventilation rates
It has proven more difficult than expected to gather investigations made by contractors of the
crawl space and organic ground slab construction. When contacting Scandi Byg in the beginning
of the thesis period they were not able to find the measurements in their own system. However,
Camilla Günther Lydersen was re-hired at Scandi Byg from 1st of January 2025 and was able
to locate the measurements and ventilation investigations. However, it is unclear whether all
data from Scandi Byg has been found. Moisture measurements provided by Scandi Byg con-
sists of three parts; continuous measurements from one constructed project, plots of continuous
measurements from a test building, and manual measurements from 13 divisions.
BM Byggeindustri has the 14th of January provided access to the one project that they are mon-
itoring. The access is provided through the Tector website [92]. Furthermore, manual measure-
ments from one project was also provided.
It is stated in the interviews by both Lejerbo and KAB, that they do not monitor the constructions.

5.3.2
Investigations of ventilation rates
Scandi Byg also provided two reports of investigations of ventilation rate beneath the suspended
floor; one for two test buildings, see section 5.3.2.1, and one for the division Kløverbladsgade, see
section 5.3.2.2. The reports are written by Bygge- og Miljøteknik A/S and Seierøe Byggeteknik
ApS respectively, and can be seen in Appendix F.3 and Appendix F.4.
Both reports investigate whether the ventilation meets the recommendations for strong venti-
lation under pavilion buildings, similar to the requirements for slabs above ground in pavilion
construction [32]. Tests have been conducted using smoke.
The ventilation is described in the reports in Appendix F.3 and F.4, as being achieved through a
20 cm wide open grille, where the opening area is reduced to approximately 10 cm due to the
profile supporting the grilles. The ventilation air is directed vertically down through the grille to
the bottom side of the box modules, which are located 30 cm below ground level. From there the
air moves horizontally under the box modules in a 20 cm high cavity before being discharged on
the opposite side. Therefore, the air passes through two 90-degree bends.
5.3.2.1
Ventilation investigations of test buildings
The building has the ventilation grille along the full length of both facades. The wind speed at
the day was around 0.5 - 1.5 m/s in the north direction. The building is oriented with its gables
facing North-South. The wind speed is considered being very weak.
The experiment shows, that there is suction along one entire facade and discharge along the
other facade.
The experiment was done three times, and the average air change rate was 85 ach, which is a
very high air change rate, and it is assessed that the ventilation beneath the building complies
with the recommendations for pavilions.
5.3.2.2
Ventilation investigation at Kløverbladsgade
Three blocks in Kløverbladsgade were tested. The buildings have the ventilation grille along the
full length of both facades as well as around half of the lengths of the gables. It is estimated
that the opening area is 1000 cm2 per meter of exterior wall, whereas the recommended area
is 150 cm2 per meter of exterior wall. The ventilation area is therefore 7 times larger than the
recommended. The wind speed at the day was around 0.2 - 1.8 m/s, which is considered being
very weak.
The experiment was done three times for each of the three blocks, and the average air change
rates were 116 ach, 55 ach, and 186 ach, which are high air change rates, and it is assessed that
the ventilation beneath the building complies with the recommendations for pavilions.
5.3.2.3
Summary
The ventilation investigations show that the the crawl spaces are very highly ventilated, with air
change rates of at least 55 ach on wind-still days.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
83

WUFI® simulations
The results from the simulations are shown in tables in sections 5.4.1 to 5.4.6 for each simulation,
and the findings are summarized in section 5.4.7. Simplified illustrations of the constructions
are placed beside the tables to indicate where the construction is evaluated. For each of the
four material classes, the stabilized mould index value is listed in the table. For some of the
investigations, the mould index is evaluated high in the first year and then declining relatively
linearly, as shown in Figure 5.9 for the Spruce 455 layer in simulation F. Therefore, the results
include a ”Maximum value” row. In the description of the tables, the number of years for the
mould index to reach below 𝑀= 2.0 is specified for the worst case. This spike of mould index in
the beginning of the simulation period could be decreased by lowering the initial moisture level.
The initial moisture level for Spruce 455 (built-in moisture) was 80 kg/m3, which corresponds to
a moisture content of 17.6 MC%.
Figure 5.9: Mould index for simulation F, in the Spruce 455 layer in the bottom of the beam layer. The
sensitivity class is ”Very sensitive” and the material class is ”Almost no decline”. The maximum value is
𝑀= 2.9, and after 3 years, the mould index is below 𝑀= 2.0.
It has been chosen to evaluate the full layer of plywood or Spruce 455 in the simulation. This
was chosen, since the difference between the outside and the inside of the layers was significant.
For Spruce 455 in simulation A, the plot of the moisture level [MC%] for each of the six splits
in the layer is shown in Figure 5.10. The figure shows that the moisture level on the inside of

5.4.7
Summary of simulations
A summary of the simulations for each of the two studied constructions can be seen in section
5.4.7.1 and 5.4.7.2 respectively.
5.4.7.1
Simulations of suspended floor
Exterior surface
For simulation A, the exterior surface does not reach critical mould index levels (red), however
the mould indices are high for sensitivity class Very sensitive for all material classes (𝑀= 4.5 and
above), see Table 5.3. For sensitivity class Sensitive only material class Almost no decline evaluate
a mould index level that should be assessed (yellow), at 𝑀= 3.5.
Placing a layer of 52 mm insulation beneath the suspended floor (simulation B) increases the
mould indices, since the temperature of the surface is decreased, due to the increased thermal
resistance of the suspended floor, see Table 5.6. When the surface is evaluated as Rough, the
indices are further increased, see Table 5.7. For the sensitivity class Very sensitive, the values
range from 𝑀= 5.2 for Significant decline to 𝑀= 5.6 for Almost no decline. For the sensitivity
class Sensitive, the values are 𝑀= 4.2 for Almost no decline and 𝑀= 3.5 for Relatively low decline.
The same indices are evaluated for simulation C, where the insulation layer is increased to 104
mm.
For the exterior surface in the crawl space, there could be some accumulation of dust and natural
debris which can support mould growth. However, the surface is facing downwards, and it is
not expected that a great amount of debris can accumulate, and therefore the sensitivity class is
highly likely above Very sensitive at least. However, this should be investigated further.
Plywood board
For simulation A, the plywood board does not experience critical mould index levels (red), but
experience levels that should be assessed (yellow) for sensitivity class Very sensitive, at 𝑀= 2.3 at
highest, see Table 5.4. In WUFI® Mould Index VTT, the material Plywood (softwood) is registered
to be Sensitive and Relevant decline, which is safe (green) in the evaluation.
For simulation B and C no mould growth is experienced in the plywood board (𝑀= 0.0) for all
evaluations.
Spruce 455 layer
For simulation A, the spruce layer reaches critical mould index levels (red) at sensitivity class
Very sensitive for all material classes, at highest 𝑀= 4.5, see Table 5.5. Critical levels are also
reached for sensitivity class Sensitive for material class Almost no decline, at 𝑀= 3.1, and require
assessment (yellow) for the rest of the material classes.
Placing a layer of 52 mm insulation below the suspended floor decreases the mould indices to
𝑀= 0.0 for all evaluations. Spikes are registered in the beginning of the simulation period, but
can be reduced by reducing the built-in moisture. Increasing the layer of insulation to 104 mm
decreases the spikes as well.
5.4.7.2
Simulations of organic ground slab
For simulation D and E, the mould indices for the Spruce 455 layer in the organic ground slabs
are 𝑀= 0.0 after 1 year of simulation. The spike in the beginning of the simulation period is
𝑀= 2.4 at highest, see Table 5.14. The value of the spike is increased to 𝑀= 2.9 and drying out
time is increased to 3 years by using laminate flooring (simulation F), see Table 5.16. The spikes
can be reduced, by reducing the built-in moisture.

5.5
LCA screening
The results of the LCA screening are shown in Figure 5.25, depicting the climate emissions per
module for each construction. Result tables can be found in Appendix E.5.
Figure 5.25: Results of LCA screening for the four constructions. The sum of module A1-A3, C3 and C4
are evaluated as the total emissions, whereas module D is the potential benefits at the end of life.
The total emissions for the concrete ground slab are 2.9 kg CO2-eq/m2/year, for the organic
ground slab the emissions are 2.3 kg CO2-eq/m2/year. For the crawl space construction with
concrete pier foundation the emissions are estimated to 1.6 kg CO2-eq/m2/year, and for the crawl
space construction with screw foundation the emissions are estimated to 1.3 kg CO2-eq/m2/year.
Looking at Figure 5.25, it can be seen that module C3 and C4 (modules related to waste handling
and recycling processes) are similar for each construction type. The major differences are seen
in modules A1-A3 where emissions related to sourcing of raw-materials and production of the
specific components are created. For module D a better end of life potential can be seen for crawl
space with screw foundation, which is almost double that of the two other bio-based options, and
even more compared to the concrete ground slab.
It can be seen from the figure, that there seems to be a significant potential when using crawl
spaces compared to the traditional concrete ground slabs and organic ground slabs. Of the two
crawl space options, the solution with screw foundation appears to have lower emissions.
The summed emissions per material for module A1-A3, C3 and C4 can be seen in Figure 5.26.
90
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:103==
Figure 5.26: Results of LCA screening for the sum of the modules A1-A3, C3, C4, and D for each construc-
tion type, showing the contributions from each material or component.
The results indicate that the concrete ground slab has significantly higher CO2-emissions com-
pared to the three other options with a total of 2.9 kg CO2-eq/m2/year. This is largely due to
the large amounts of concrete needed, where the concrete for the strip foundation and load-
distributing plate total about 40% of the emissions, with an additional almost 16% coming from
the reinforcement steel embedded in the concrete. The EPS insulation is also a large contributor
accounting for almost 33% of the emissions.
The emission for the organic ground slab is lower than that of the concrete ground slab, with
a total of 2.3 kg CO2-eq/m2/year. It is the same large contributing factors, EPS account for al-
most 41% of the emissions, the concrete for the strip foundation accounts for about 26% of the
emissions, with an additional 10% coming from the reinforcement.
The crawl space with concrete pier foundation is seemingly the next best option with a total of
emissions of 1.6 kg CO2-eq/m2/year. The concrete for the pier foundation is the main contributor
to the emission accounting for around 25% with an additional 10% coming from the reinforce-
ment steel embedded in the concrete. L-elements are also significant contributors accounting for
almost 20% of the total emissions.
The crawl space with screw foundation appear to be the best performing option with a total of
emissions of 1.3 kg CO2-eq/m2/year. The main contributor are L-elements accounting for almost
25%, and the screw foundation itself accounting for almost 19% of the total emissions.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
91
==PAGE:104==
6
Discussion
This thesis has focused on modular constructions, investigating present use of highly ventilated
crawl spaces and organic ground slabs. It has not been investigated how widely crawl spaces
have been used outside of modular construction, and therefore it is not clear, to what extent
traditional cold crawl spaces or warm crawl spaces are constructed today.
The crawl spaces used in the assessed modular constructions have ventilation rates of at least 55
ach on wind-still days. The ventilation is ensured by ventilation grilles either all around facades
and gables, or at least at two opposing facades. The high air change rates ensure that moisture
evaporation from the ground does not impact the relative humidity in the crawl space, as the
moist air is rapidly ventilated from the crawl space. In the following sections various aspects of
the highly ventilated crawl space and organic ground slab constructions will be discussed.
6.1
Measurements and hygrothermal simulations
Measurements gathered from different crawl space constructions show seemingly acceptable
levels of moisture in the tested constructions. However, for most of the measurements, it is not
known exactly where the measurement points are located inside the construction, and therefore
it is not known, if the most critical parts are tested, such as the bottom of the beams, below the
plywood board or in the corners and edges of the suspended floor at the gables, where plywood
is only separated from the soil by a metal sheet. The moisture levels in the organic ground slabs
generally seems lower than those for the crawl space constructions, see Figure 5.7, and therefore
also seemingly acceptable. However, the number of unknown constructions is significant, and
therefore it cannot be concluded that moisture levels are lower in organic ground slabs compared
to crawl spaces. In the simulations, lower moisture levels were calculated for the organic ground
slab compared to the crawl space as well.
At one sensor at Teglmosen, where the choice of construction towards terrain is unknown, the
measurements were above 20 MC% for both sensors used. This could be due to an isolated inci-
dent, such as operational activities or another acute event. This location is not measured again,
therefore it is not known if the moisture level has decreased below the critical point. It is not
known if any remedial measures were taken.
The measurements imply that both constructions can perform acceptable, if they are designed
and constructed correctly. However, all measurements provided have been conducted in the
winter period, and very few in the beginning of the summer period, and further only one covering
a continuous period including summers and autumn. Critical levels of moisture are expected
more in the summer, and therefore more measurements should be conducted in this period.
Hygrothermal simulations of three different crawl space constructions show that critical mois-
ture levels inside the construction, and therefore risk of mould growth, can be avoided by placing
a layer of 50 mm insulation beneath the beam layer. This result was also found through measure-
ments in the article ”Moisture related problems in highly insulated constructions” in the literature
review [39]. Placing 100 mm insulation instead of 50 mm does not affect the simulation results,
which indicates that 50 mm is enough. This shows that the SBi guidelines are on the safe side,
by advising for 100 mm of insulation beneath the suspended floor.
It is also recommended in SBi guidelines to use an inorganic board below the beam layer, however
the simulations have shown that acceptable moisture levels are achieved in the plywood board
used instead, when 50 mm of insulation is placed below it. This indicates that this could be a safe
92
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:105==
construction, and an inorganic board may not be necessary. This should be investigated further
by measurements.
The simulations of the crawl space show a mould index as high as 𝑀= 5.6 calculated for the ex-
terior surface for simulations with 50 mm and 100 mm insulation below the suspended floor. The
realistic sensitivity class for the exterior surface is not known, but is likely relatively sensitive,
since this would be the dust and debris accumulated at the surface. It should be investigated fur-
ther to which extent this accumulation is happening, and which sensitivity class it would put the
exterior surface in. However, this can be difficult to investigate, since the crawl spaces are not
accessible for inspection and mould samples, due to the low height of the cavity. However, these
results show that wood fiber insulation would not be a good choice for the exterior insulation,
since the sensitivity class for this material would be either Very sensitive or Sensitive.
In general it is a significant simplification to simulate a ground slab or crawl space construction
as a one-dimensional model. This only gives a limited presentation of the reality. The simula-
tions do not cover the risks associated with the edges, joints or other details. Furthermore, the
simulations do not include the risks of damages or errors in craftsmanship; for instance leaking
pipes or water in other ways penetrating into the construction. Furthermore, it is not assessed
whether a construction can dry out if water has come into the construction. Also, the exte-
rior conditions are getting more extreme due to climate changes, which should be considered in
future simulations as well.
It is shown in the calibration, that choice of material plays an important role when evaluating.
Therefore, it is important to know more specifically which materials are used in the construction
to assess the best material fit in WUFI®, and ensure the most realistic simulation. Due to the
calibration, it is expected that the simulations are predicting more critical moisture levels than
what is reality. However, the utilized climate Bremerhaven is found to be assessed less critical
than the Danish conditions, which is not on the safe side. This shows the importance of moisture
measurements, to validate simulations. The simulations suggest that both suspended floors with
52 mm insulation beneath and organic ground slab constructions are moisture-safe, however this
should be confirmed by more continuous measurements.
The high ventilation rate in these types of crawl spaces has caused the modelling choice of using
the outside weather as exterior conditions in the simulations. If however it is found that other
types of crawl spaces are constructed, with lower ventilation rates, such as in the span of 1-2 ach
for traditional crawl spaces, the thermal lag should be included in the simulations. This requires
development of a simulation tool able to calculate the conditions in the crawl space, when warm
air enter the crawl space and is cooled down, causing a rise in relative humidity.
6.2
Life-Cycle Analysis screening
Based on the life-cycle analysis screening, the crawl space construction seems to have a sig-
nificant advantage over the organic ground slab. The organic ground slab also show reduction
potential compared to the concrete ground slab. In the LCA screening only CO2-eq emissions
are taken into account. It should be noted that other factors are important to assess as well, such
as bio-diversity and land use, which might be negatively affected by using bio-based materials.
This evaluation is not included in this thesis, since the building regulations in Denmark only
require calculations of CO2-eq emissions at present.
Furthermore, since the LCA is a screening including only the A1-A3, C3, C4 and D modules, it
must be taken into account that the construction process (A5) of the four assessed construction
types will vary as well. This was not taken into account in the presented calculations, decreasing
the fairness of the comparison. Furthermore, material quantities are based on approximations
and do not include various joints and smaller details. Also, it was found that the EPS insulation
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
93
==PAGE:106==
plays a significant part of the emissions. The screening is based on the solutions having the same
U-value. This is however not taking into account that the exterior conditions will be different
between the ground slab constructions and suspended floor constructions. The SBi Guideline
279 mentions, that highly ventilated crawl spaces are less energy-efficient than other types of
crawl spaces. The suspended floor construction will lead to higher heat losses than organic
ground slabs, since the thermal capacity of the soil is not included and the outside conditions
have lower temperatures than the soil. For the constructions to have the same heat loss, either
more insulation should be placed in the suspended floor slab, or less EPS in the ground slabs.
Limiting the use of EPS in the ground slab would have a significant positive impact on the CO2-
eq emissions for this construction. If the constructions have different heat loss, it will impact
emissions in module B1-7 as more energy is needed to obtain comfort temperatures for the crawl
space solution.
The concrete L-elements are a significant factor in the LCA screening as well. Therefore, it could
be investigated if there are other, less CO2-eq demanding solutions, that still effectively retain
the soil.
6.3
Organic material below terrain
In the common technical knowledge the highly ventilated crawl space below terrain and organic
ground slab are not described, since they are both placed down into the terrain. However, it
seems like there is a difference of opinion on this matter, with the contractors arguing that the
plinth trench ensure compliance with experience leaflet ”Level access and ground slabs”, since the
membrane on top of the EPS insulation is more than 50 mm above the ”surrounding terrain”,
which is the bottom of the plinth trench. This is also accepted by Byggeskadefonden in the case
of Gartnergården.
For the crawl space construction it appears that some areas of the construction may not meet the
requirements, as the only separation between organic material and the soil is a metal sheet at the
gable. A significant concern is the tightness and corrosion class of these metal sheets embedded
in the soil. If the corrosion class is not adequate, the lifespan is reduced which poses significant
risks to the construction. Also, there is a risk of not sealing the joint between the sheets properly.
If however the whole building is surrounded by a plinth trench, this issue is avoided. It has not
been discovered why this solution was not chosen for all assessed buildings with crawl spaces.
This could be due to geometric challenges, which must be investigated further and taken into
account as well.
The issue of moisture damages in bio-based constructions towards terrain are caused by the re-
quirement for level access. Perhaps it might be worth considering whether this requirement can
be reduced or differentiated, especially in light of the need for a green transition of the building
sector. Each building could be assessed to determine if level access is necessary for the use of
the building. Instead, crawl spaces above ground could be constructed, which would limit the
risks of moisture damages quite significantly. There is already a diversification in the use of
buildings in social housing, for instance either senior or youth housing. Additionally, in most
AlmenBolig+ cases, the first floor is only accessible by stairs, already limiting the accessibil-
ity. This cost might be necessary to construct more sustainable buildings and limit the risk of
moisture damages, which are costly both economically and environmentally. However, this also
influences the flexibility of the buildings.
6.4
Drainage design and moisture loads
It is important to ensure that proper site investigations are conducted before designing the con-
struction. Planning the surrounding area is crucial for bio-based structures towards terrain. It
94
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:107==
must be assessed whether the area is suitable for such constructions, and to ensure sufficient
drainage of the terrain. If the terrain is not suitable for bio-based constructions, alternative solu-
tions must be considered. Future climate changes are expected to increase precipitation and raise
groundwater levels. This must be considered when designing constructions towards terrain, as
increased moisture load from precipitation and groundwater can cause significant damages, even
from small mistakes in craftsmanship. Therefore, rising groundwater levels must also be consid-
ered when choosing the location and designing the surrounding area.
Bio-based constructions are generally less impacting on the environment, however if placed in
areas that are not suitable, the risk of damages increases. If damage occurs, repairs are costly
both economically and in terms of CO2-eq emissions. Placed in higher terrain, these construction
are more safe.
It is also important to make moisture-safe decisions during the construction phase to protect the
organic constructions. Temporary solutions needs to be considered, such as how to divert rain-
water. The ”Built-in-Wood Demonstrator” is an unfortunate example. The water pipe installed
inside the building was not properly designed to handle the heavy loads of rainwater accumu-
lated on the roof, which led to the bursting of the pipe. It is not advisable to install rainwater
pipes inside buildings. Additionally, high levels of water accumulated inside the crawl space,
because proper drainage on the terrain was not ensured during construction.
6.5
Moisture measurements
In this thesis, it has turned out that the measurements of the constructions were not as well
managed as one could hope, and perhaps not in line with the expectations of Byggeskadefonden.
The analysis of registrations from Byggeskadefonden imply that there is an expectation that the
building administrators monitor the constructions. However, this is not actually the case. At
the 5-year inspections, none of the constructions indicate that they are being monitored, even
though it is required for several of them. Also, the manual measurements show, that moisture
levels were measured only once or twice at every building, even though it is recommend to do
measurements at least two times a year.
The contractors indicated that they install moisture sensors that operate continuously, however
it was harder than expected to acquire these measurements. BM Byggeindustri only monitor one
construction, and Scandi Byg could not locate the measurements for a long time, and therefore it
can be assumed, that they do not monitor the constructions any more. Also, the administrators
stated that they do not monitor the constructions. It is important that building owners or admin-
istrators know they need to monitor the buildings themselves, or make a service agreement with
the contractor or a third party to manage the task, and effectively monitor the constructions, but
this can be an expensive solution.
It has been found in the interviews, that there is a conflict of how Byggeskadefonden and KAB in-
terpret the requirements for moisture measurements stated in the registrations. KAB have stated
that they view it as recommendations, while Byggeskadefonden consider it mandatory proving
that moisture investigations have been made and the construction have been monitored, to re-
ceive compensation for damages. It could be advised for Byggeskadefonden to require even more
clearly through dialog, that the constructions must be more closely monitored. Perhaps it should
be required to report the moisture measurements more often than only at the 1- and 5-year reg-
istrations and at potential damages. This could ensure that moisture measurements are made
frequently. Byggeskadefonden could have closer cooperation with the contractors and adminis-
trators on having moisture sensors built-in to the constructions, and coordinate the monitoring.
Possibly it would be of value to centralize the monitoring of the constructions; perhaps it is of
interest to Byggeskadefonden to do so, so knowledge can be easily compiled and shared. In any
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
95
==PAGE:108==
case, it is an issue that needs to be addressed, and it should be determined how this can be done
effectively to ensure that any damage is detected as soon as possible. The most critical points
in the construction should be identified and monitored. As more constructions are monitored in
various locations, the number of required monitoring positions can potentially be reduced.
6.6
Maintenance
In the AlmenBolig+ projects in KAB, residents are responsible for the maintenance of the build-
ings themselves to keep the costs of the apartments low. Communication regarding the impor-
tance of maintenance of certain constructions might be missed, as residents frequently move in
and out and often lack the expert knowledge needed to make observations and take preventive
actions. This is particularly problematic when the constructions are difficult or impossible to
inspect, such as the studied constructions towards terrain. Clear instructions in the operations
and maintenance plan and the delivery from project engineers and contractors to administrators
is key. It is clear in the case of Skovkvarteret, that the administrators as well as the project man-
ager from KAB are unaware of the requirements for moisture measurements. This issue might
be mentioned in the operations and maintenance plan, but there is a risk that it is not being
carried out either way. Therefore, continuous moisture measurements, which do not require in-
volvement of residents or KAB administrators, are crucial. Furthermore, it is important to have
a system in place to support this, rather than relying on individual persons, which could make
the monitoring vulnerable.
It is required by SBi guidelines that crawl spaces are accessible for maintenance and inspection
of installations, by either sufficient height or by hatches in the floor. This is not complied with,
since the crawl space, the suspended floor, and the organic ground slab are all inaccessible for
investigations of installations and tests of mould. Therefore, it is difficult to detect if damage has
occurred. It can only be discovered through destructive investigation or if residents report issues
of smell or uneven floors. It can be a concern, that if issues are not actively sought out, damages
might be discovered too late to be effectively remedied. Some of these concerns can be adressed
by installing moisture sensors.
It is important that the moisture-safety of the constructions do not depend on pumps. There is a
possibility of the pumps being out of use at critical times, for instance because residents unknow-
ingly shut them off, or in cases of cloud bursts, if the water pressure is too heavy and causes the
pumps to break. KAB notes that crawl space constructions have an advantage, as water can be
pumped out if flooding is detected before it reaches the structure and causes damage. However,
this issue can be difficult to identify without an alert system to notify of water accumulation in
the crawl space, which is currently not in place. A solution for this could be considered investi-
gated further. A safer alternative to pumps is the principle of gravitation, which do not rely on
the operation and maintenance in the same way. It must be ensured that water getting into the
crawl space at cloud bursts can easily get out, and preferably not by use of pumps. The cavity
below the suspended floor gives some resilience for the crawl space compared to the organic
ground slab, but it cannot be relied for as a solution for the safety of the construction. It is of
importance that local handling of surface water is adequately designed to handle current and
future precipitation loads. The issue is however, that the solution can become inefficient in the
case of residents altering the terrain for their own use.
A problem, especially for the organic ground slab, is the maintenance of the plinth trench. It
should be written in the operations and maintenance plan that it has to be emptied and cleaned
once a year. It is not known if this is complied with in the housing divisions. Furthermore, it
can be a concern, if the plinth trenches are filled with gravel, as this causes the trench to lose
its function of diverting water. This has been observed for Gartnergården, and can therefore be
considered a valid concern. In some cases for the crawl space construction, it has been found
96
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:109==
that residents cover the ventilation grille, probably unknowingly. If this occurs along the entire
facade, it can limit ventilation. It must be communicated to the residents, probably on a regular
basis, that it is important to not cover the ventilation grille. However, the ventilation rates are
very high, so it can be assumed that covering parts of the grille will not reduce ventilation below
the necessary level.
For both constructions it is important to ensure that the seals below doors and windows are tight,
and it is important to maintain these throughout the lifetime of the building, so water does not
penetrate to the construction.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
97
==PAGE:110==
7
Conclusion
The current recommendations for insulation thicknesses in building envelopes have resulted
in unacceptable high levels of relative humidity in traditional crawl spaces, leading to mould
growth. Therefore, concrete ground slab are more widely used at present. However, due to
human-induced climate change, there is a growing desire to build more bio-based structures to
reduce CO2-eq emissions. In this thesis a focus has been on organic modular constructions,
where both highly ventilated crawl space and organic ground slab constructions have been used.
The crawl space solutions used, has been found by others to be very highly ventilated, at air
change rates of at least 55 ach at wind-still days.
Constructions, such as crawl spaces and organic ground slabs, have been shown in this thesis to
have reduced CO2-eq emissions compared to concrete ground slab. The emissions for a crawl
space with concrete pier foundation are less than for an organic ground slab, and the emissions
are reduced even further when using a crawl space solution with screw foundations.
Organic constructions are more susceptible to moisture issues than mineral based constructions.
Adding to this issue, accessibility regulations in Denmark require level access to all buildings.
This results in buildings constructed into the terrain, which increases the risk of moisture dam-
ages such as mould growth. It could perhaps be considered to differentiate level access to build-
ings, in order to be able to construct completely moisture-safe crawl space constructions above
terrain.
There are concerns for the moisture-safety of both crawl spaces and organic ground slabs. The
concerns of the crawl space is mostly, that organic materials are not protected sufficiently from
direct contact with soil in some of the periphery of the building. There can be joints constructed
badly with sheets of metal being the only separation between organic materials in the construc-
tion and soil in the gables. This can be remedied by ensuring that the crawl space is ventilated
at all gables and facades around the building. There could however be reasons to why this was
not used already, such as geometrical challenges.
Simulations of the suspended floor in the crawl space construction showed that mould growth
can be avoided by placing a layer of 50 mm inorganic insulation beneath the beam layer. An-
other concern for the crawl space is the use of a plywood board as wind barrier instead of an
inorganic board, as recommended in the required technical knowledge. The simulations indicate
that plywood boards can be used.
Simulations for the organic ground slab show that no mould growth is expected. The main
concern for the organic ground slab is the maintenance of the construction, which is found to
be more critical for the moisture-safety, than the maintenance for the crawl space. The drainage
and cleaning of the plinth trenches is crucial, and it is important that the drainage does not rely
on pumps. Also, in the construction phase it is crucial that the radon barrier is tight, especially
around installations, and that the building module is placed on dry foundations.
The simulations for both constructions assume perfect constructions, and are calculated in one
dimension, therefore more investigations should be made, such as moisture measurements. It
was found in this thesis, that the constructions are not as closely monitored, as Byggeskade-
fonden requires them to be. This shows the need for a change in the view of the maintenance
of these constructions and possibly a re-structure of the system. Future moisture measurements
could possibly be gathered at one institution and the monitoring centralized, so that an overview
98
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:111==
of the well-functioning of the constructions can be obtained, or it could be required to report mea-
surements yearly to Byggeskadefonden, in order to ensure that the constructions are monitored.
The latter solution places high demands on the administrators, who, in some social housing as-
sociations, are the residents themselves. Furthermore, the requirements for conducting moisture
measurements need to be even more clear, as it seems to be interpreted differently between insti-
tutions. This should be addressed before the construction of the building, as the moisture sensors
should be integrated in the construction.
For both constructions, the design of the surrounding terrain is crucial, to ensure that water
is diverted away from the building. A concern for both constructions is the resilience against
future climate changes, including more often and severe cloud bursts, and rising ground water
levels. It can be argued that the crawl space solution is more resilient than the organic ground
slab solution, since any potential flooding can be addressed before the water makes significant
damage in the construction. However, it is difficult to detect that the crawl space is filled with
water, as it is below ground level, and no mechanism is in place to alert for high levels of water
in the crawl space.
In order to build moisture-safe organic constructions towards terrain, a few important issues has
to be addressed, some of the most important being; the location of the building in suitable terrain,
drainage by gravitational principle, ensuring that the organic construction cannot be in direct
contact with soil, and monitoring the construction by moisture measurements more efficiently.
It is very important that there is an increased focus on the maintenance of constructions towards
terrain.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
99
==PAGE:112==
8
Further research
In this thesis it has been found that a few issues should be investigated further, which are sum-
marized in the list below.
• Further work with simulations:
– Simulations with the Danish Design Reference Year as exterior conditions, to ensure
that the constructions can withstand these conditions.
– Simulations with projected future climate.
– Simulations of the constructions in two dimensions in the corners of the building, to
investigate if this affects the safety of the organic materials.
– Simulate drying process of water damages with various flooring types, to assess the
resilience of the constructions.
• Further work with moisture measurements:
– Compile more existing data. Build-in moisture measurements in new constructions
and conduct more investigations of moisture and temperature conditions in sus-
pended floors and organic ground slabs. Measurements in the summer are crucial.
Conduct measurements in the plywood boards in the suspended floors specifically,
to assess if the recommendation can be changed from inorganic boards.
– Investigation of drying process of building moisture by integrating sensors during
the production of the modules.
– Identify the areas where the highest levels of moisture are expected, and compile a
list of crucial measurement points.
• Investigate why the plinth trench solution is not applied at all gables and facades for build-
ings with crawl space constructions. This might be due to geometric challenges. Identify
ways to implement this solution.
• Investigate the accumulation of debris on the bottom of the suspended floor.
• Further investigation of other crawl space construction outside social housing projects.
• Identify a more CO2-efficient alternative to L-elements for retaining soil.
• Investigations of moisture related issues and risks in the construction phase.
• Investigations of air change rates in crawl spaces, to ensure that for all new constructions
the ventilation rate is high. If the ventilation rate is not sufficiently high, this affects the
moisture levels in the crawl space, which will rise more that the outdoor conditions, and
then the assumptions in this thesis do not apply.
• Investigations of surface transfer coefficients applicable for crawl spaces, depending on the
air change rate.
100
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:121==
B
Build-in-Wood Demonstrator
B.1
Description of the building
The ”Build-in-Wood Demonstrator” is a test and demonstration facility placed at Danish Techno-
logical Institute (DTI). It has been under construction since February of 2024 to the start of 2025.
It is an EU-supported Horizon 2020 project, and is a collaboration of over 20 partners working
with different parts of the wood construction sector [74]. It is a two storey demonstration build-
ing, and the construction is based on glulam columns and beam, CLT floor slabs for the first floor
and lightweight wooden facade elements [93]. An illustration of the structure can be seen in
Figure B.1a and a rendering of the building is shown in Figure B.1b, both pictures are from the
Build-in-Wood site [74]. The part of the building with the screw foundation and floor beams,
shown in Figure B.1a, is the test part of the building, and a crawl space is used here.
(a) Illustration of the structure of the building, by
[74].
(b) Rendering of the building, by [74].
Figure B.1: Illustrations of the Build-in-Wood Demonstrator.
The idea of the building is to be modular, which means that the elements can be taken out and
replaced by other elements, in order to test these elements in real environments [74]. The produc-
ers of wood products can evaluate their innovative building materials, components and systems
in the real world [74].
In this thesis the focus is on the suspended floor and crawl space below. The modules here
are equipped with sensors, in multiple places and levels in the construction, which is shown
in subsection B.2.1. The sensors measure the temperature and humidity of the wooden beams
and the relative humidity of the air in the layers in the cassettes (which is equal to the relative
humidity of the material between the wooden beams, i.e. insulation).
B.1.1
Constructions
As a test two different constructions for the suspended floor are installed in the North and South
part of the test part of the building. The Northern construction is inorganic, as shown in Fig-
ure B.2a and the Southern part is organic as shown in Figure B.2b.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
iii
==PAGE:122==
(a) The construction of the floor cassettes with use of mineral wool insulation.
(b) The construction of the floor cassettes with use of wood fiber insulation.
Figure B.2: The construction of the floor cassettes in the test part of the building.
It should be noted that until around the start of November 2024, the inorganic construction, did
not have the bottom layer of 45 mm insulation installed.
Figure B.3 shows an illustration of the crawl space during construction. There is a slope towards
the bottom of the crawl space near the openings. When the construction is finished the ground
should be level all around the building, and sloping away from the building. The foundation is
made with ground screws, and around the perimeter of the test part of the building, concrete
elements are placed to retain the soil.
Figure B.3: Illustration of the crawl space during construction.
The crawl space is lowered into the ground, in order to comply with the requirement for level
access. Therefore, the part of the building that includes a crawl space, is surrounded by a concrete
retaining wall.
B.1.2
Ventilation conditions
In the construction phase the crawl space was very well ventilated, as there were two large
openings, in the East and North facades, placed as shown in Figure B.4. The openings have a
width of 1.14 m and a height of 0.80 m.
B.2
Sensors
B.2.1
Placement of sensors
The placements of the sensors in the floor cassettes are shown in Figure B.4b, where the letters V,
M and K, describe which layer the sensor is placed in. V is at the warm side of the construction,
K is at the cold side and M is in the middle. The placements of V and K can be seen in Figure B.5b.
Furthermore, sensors have been placed on wooden columns around the crawl space, to measure
humidity and temperature in the crawl space just below the suspended floor. The sensors are
iv
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:123==
located as shown in Figure B.4a. Pictures of the placements in the crawl space can be seen in
Figure B.5a.
(a) Placement of the sensors in the crawl space.
(b) Placement of the sensors in the construction. V
is on the warm side, K is on the cold side and M is in
the middle. KM shows manual measuring point on
the cold side.
Figure B.4: Placement of sensors in TI Demonstrator Building.
(a) Placement of the sensors in the crawl space. The
sensors are placed in the top of wooden columns.
(b) Placement of a sensor in the cold side and the
warm side of the construction.
Figure B.5: Pictures of placements of the sensors.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
v
==PAGE:124==
B.2.2
Tector sensors
The sensors used are Tector sensors [94]. The sensors have been installed in the floor cassettes
by Enemærke & Petersen, at their factory site, and the moisture measurements should there-
fore contain data from the manufacturing to the construction phase, and after the building is in
use. The sensors are mounted in the desired layer with isolated screws, as shown in Figure B.5.
Measurements of wood moisture content, humidity and temperature are recorded around every
hour. The sensors are wireless connected through LoRaWAN [94].
The sensor accuracy is 0.3% for wood moisture content in the 7-27% range and 2% for relative
humidity in the 10-90% range. Measurements beyond these ranges have a reduced accuracy [94].
The sensors log the room temperature and humidity, by intake and discharge of air through the
top and bottom of the sensor. The humidity and temperature of the specimen which the sensor
is placed in is found through measurements of resistance between the conductive screws.
(a) Woodsense sensor.
(b) Inside the sensor.
Figure B.6: The Woodsense sensor.
Continues measurements of relative humidity and temperature is carried out in the crawl space
and in the floor cassettes. Furthermore, the indoor climate was measured.
The measurements in the crawl space were conducted on wooden columns, that allowed for the
measurements to be made just below the suspended floor.
The measurements in the crawl space were validated with measurements with a Testo datalogger,
which was checked in a climate chamber with conditions 50%/23∘C.
B.2.3
Data processing
The data (temperature, relative humidity, wood moisture, and weather data) is gathered and
accessed through the Tector.com website [92]. The data is downloaded for the selected time
period in Microsoft Excel. Two macros have been developed, to sort and plot the data. The
first macro sort the mixed data into worksheets for each sensor, and the second macro plots the
relative humidity and temperature with respect to time. The macros can be seen in Appendix D.
The same macros are used for the crawl space and the sensors in the cassettes, but only shown
for the crawl space in the appendix.

Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

B.3
Mould investigations
Agar investigations for mould were made in four different locations; two on each bottom ma-
terial, shown in Figure B.2, in three different locations in the crawl space. The samples were
taken the 2nd of October and 4th of December. The samples are made by pressing the Agar
plates towards the bottom side of the suspended floor. In this way the potential mould spores
are collected.

B.3.1
Results
The growth on the Agar plates are evaluated on the basis of the number of the colonies. This
evaluation is done by TI. The results from TI can be seen in Figure B.9 and Figure B.10.
Figure B.9: Results from the mould investigation at 2nd of October 2024.
Figure B.10: Results from the mould investigation at 4th of December 2024.
The results of the mould tests performed both on the 2nd of October and 4th of December showed
no clear indication of mould growth, but rather an accumulation of spores in the dust at the
sampling sites.
B.4
Heavy rainfall
In the days 25th and 26th of September 2024 there was a heavy rain storm in the area of the
building, which caused great damages to the building. A rainwater pipe inside the building
burst, which led too huge amounts of water coming inside the building. See the flooding of the
building in Figure B.11a, and the rebuild rainwater pipe in Figure B.11b.

nat (DTI) the 26th of September 2024.
B.4.1
Flooding of floor cassettes
The contents of the bottom floor cassettes on the East and West side of the test part of the building
had to be replaced, since the material was too wet, see Figure B.12. Due to many sensors being
installed in the floor construction, it was possible to leave quite a few of the cassettes untouched,
since the humidity did not reach critical levels in these areas.

B.4.2
Flooding of crawl space
The large amounts of rain water also meant that the crawl space got flooded, since the water
could not run off away from the building. A few days before the rainstorm, the sensors were
placed in the crawl space. They became completely wet, and more of them were floating about
in the water and became relocated. Therefore, they had to be disassembled and dried out before
reinstalling, see Figure B.6b. The reinstalling happened at the 2nd of October 2024. See the
flooding of the crawl space in Figure B.16a. The crawl space were emptied by pumping, see
Figure B.16b.
x
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:129==
(a) Flooding of the crawl space.
(b) Pump for the crawl space.
Figure B.16: Flooding of the Build-in-Wood Demonstrator building, left picture is taken by Joanna Schalnat
(DTI) the 26th of September 2024.
B.4.3
Drying out approach
An interview was conducted with Joanna Schalnat from DTI, to hear about their drying out
approach. As a first approach to the water damage, the cassettes were opened. Due to many
sensors being placed in the floor cassettes, it was easily assessed if the cassettes were experiencing
damaging high levels of moisture.
The beams were also manually measured for moisture by DTI. The critical points were found to
be near the ends of the beams, since the water could be absorb easily in the longitudinal direction.
So even though it was a acute damage, the wood became quite moist also in the deeper layers of
the beam.
The cassettes that were assessed to be damaged were opened and insulation removed to ensure
the beams could dry sufficiently fast. It was ensured that there was enough ventilation, so the
moisture could evaporate to the air. Later on heat was added to speed up the process. However,
it was important for DTI to make sure that the heat was not too harsh, since this could cause
damage to the wood, by either provoking cracking or the outer layer could be dried out and
trapping the moisture inside the beam. The temperature was aimed (ideally) at around 5 degrees
higher than the normal temperature in the room.
When the wood moisture was below 18 MC% the cassettes could be filled with new insulation
and closed of. If it was done at higher levels of moisture, TI assesses that the risk of mould and
rot is present.
B.5
Measurements in the crawl space
Measurements in the crawl space were made after the flooding from the 3rd of October 2024 to
the 11th of January 2025. The measurements are showed in Figure B.17 for the temperature and
Figure B.18 for the relative humidity. The measurements show that the conditions in the crawl
space follow the outside conditions, without a lag. The crawl space was in the whole period very
well ventilated, due to the large openings.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
xi
==PAGE:130==
Figure B.17: Temperature measurements in the crawl space for all sensors, plotted together with the
outside temperature. It can be seen that the temperature inside the crawl space follows the outside.
Figure B.18: Relative humidity measurements in the crawl space for all sensors, plotted together with
the outside relative humidity. It can be seen that the relative humidity inside the crawl space follows the
outside.
There is an offset for some of the measurements for the relative humidity, which can be explained
by the sensors not being calibrated beforehand, and possibly also that some of them became
completely wet in the flooding. The sensors that showed the highest and lowest values of relative
humidity (607 and 521 respectively) were tested with the Testo datalogger, which proved this
offset for sensor 521. The logger was fist placed at sensor 607 from 25th of November to 13th of
December 2024 and afterwards at sensor 521 until the 7th of January 2025. The measurements
of sensor 607, sensor 521 and the data logger can be seen in Figure B.19 and Figure B.20.
xii
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:131==
Figure B.19: Test of temperature measurements by Testo datalogger. The tested sensors are 607 and 521.
Figure B.20: Test of relative humidity measurements by Testo datalogger. The tested sensors are 607 and
521. In the period where the Testo datalogger is placed at the same location as sensor 521 there is an offset,
which shows that the 521 sensor does not measure correctly.
B.6
Further work
Due to the water damage, the building could not be used for validation of the WUFI calculations,
as was the initial plan.
Further work could be to simulate the drying of the constructions, as to see if it can be predicted
how long time it will take.
If the sensors keep running in the crawl space it could be interesting to investigate the ventilation
rate when the crawl space is closed as it is going to be during the use of the building. The crawl
space was not closed in time to make the measurements for this thesis. It could be interesting to
see if there is a big difference between the open and closed conditions on the measurements of
the sensors in the crawl space and the ventilation rate.
For the sensors in the construction it will be interesting to see the development.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain

C.1.2
Evaluation of calibration
The ventilation in the crawl space is not known, and therefore the surface transfer coefficient
can be difficult to estimate. Therefore, two surface transfer coefficients have been tested; 𝑅𝑠𝑒=
0.17 m2K/W and 𝑅𝑠𝑒= 0.04 m2K/W. The resulting graphs from this investigation are shown in
Figure C.10, for the sandwich construction with Spruce 455.
Figure C.10: Test of surface transfer coefficients for sandwich construction with Spruce 455.
Furthermore, it is tested whether a layer of air beneath the construction will have influence on
the results. The construction with air beneath the suspended floor is shown in Figure C.11.
xviii
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
==PAGE:137==
Figure C.11: Sandwich construction with Spruce 455 with air beneath.
The results for the sandwich construction with Spruce 455 with and without air beneath the
construction is shown in Figure C.12.
Figure C.12: Test of layer of air beneath the construction for sandwich construction with Spruce 455.
It can be seen that changing the surface transfer coefficient or placing a layer of air beneath the
construction does not influence the results.
As a last test the windbarrier beneath the construction is tested in two ways; a new material is
used Asphalt Impregnated Paper (30 min Paper) and for the original material Wood fibre board,
hard - wind barrier the water vapour diffusion resistance factor is changed to a very low value
of 𝜇= 0.001. This is done, since the wind barrier should be vapour permeable. The results
can be seen in Figure C.13 together with the measurements and the results from the sandwich
construction with Spruce 455.
Moisture conditions in bio-based floor structures close to terrain
xix
==PAGE:138==
Figure C.13: Test of wind barrier beneath construction for sandwich construction with Spruce 455.
It can be seen that it does not have impact what material below the construction is chosen.

Spruce 600 as shown for Spruce 455 in the report.

Sub RMS_Sinuskurve_radata_sammenligning()
2
Dim ws As Worksheet
3
Set ws = ActiveSheet ' Brug den aktive fane
4
5
Dim lastRow As Long
6
lastRow = ws.Cells(ws.Rows.Count, "B").End(xlUp).Row ' Finder sidste række
i kolonne B
7
8
Dim sumSquares As Double
9
sumSquares = 0
10
11
' Regn RMSE for kolonnerne der ønskes ved at ændre værdierne "3" og "2"
12
Dim i As Long
13
For i = 9 To lastRow
14
If IsNumeric(ws.Cells(i, 3).value) And IsNumeric(ws.Cells(i, 2).value)
Then
15
sumSquares = sumSquares + (ws.Cells(i, 3).value - ws.Cells(i, 2).
value) ^ 2
16
Else
17
MsgBox "Non-numeric value found at row " & i
18
Exit Sub
19
End If
20
Next i
21
22
Dim rms As Double
23
rms = Sqr(sumSquares / (lastRow - 8)) ' Justerer for at starte fra række 9
24
25
ws.Range("G11").value = rms ' Printer RMS-værdien i celle G11
26
End Sub

BMT-Notat 
 
 
Almen Bolig +, KAB 
N3415-001(2) 
 
 
Udarbejdet af: CG
